On Apr 30, 2008, at 11:41 PM, robinson-west user wrote:
> If Nina is found alive...
>
> I'm ashamed of the jurors, there doesn't seem to be any solid
> evidence to prove that Nina died let alone that there was a
> murder.  The whole thing stinks.
>
> I guess the moral of the trial is, don't act guilty even if
> all the evidence against you is circumstantial.

Don't:
1. Try to hide your car.
2. Try to "clean" your car by hosing down the interior. With a hose.
3. Remove passenger seat from said car, so you "can sleep there",  
especially if you have a house and a bed.
4. Tell a jury you'd rather sleep in an undrained inch of water in  
your car than your bed, ....in a car parked a mile from your house.
5. Tell a jury that a 6-inch blood stain on a bag was caused by sex.
6. Leave bloodstains from the wife in the house, during a messy divorce.
7. Take a road trip through the desert, for no appreciable reason,  
after your wife disappears.
8. Show up to pick up your kids on the wrong day, after she  
disappears, but before there's any reason to think she wouldn't appear.

My take is that he thought he was smarter than any one cop, lawyer,  
or prosecutor he would face, and forgot about the intelligence of  
crowds.

I will miss his code contributions... but he seems guilty as heck to me.

-Bop

Not saying Hans Reiser is an example of this, as I haven't personally 
followed this case closely enough to know. However, before making a 
blanket statement that circumstantial evidence shouldn't be used as the 
basis for conviction I would do specific research on other cases where 
no physical evidence can be found, and the difficulty law enforcement 
has in even investigating, much less prosecuting them. Marilee Strong 
has written a book on cases that seem similar to Reiser's:

http://www.powells.com/biblio/1-9780787996390-0

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Thu May  1 09:27:36 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Thu, 01 May 2008 10:27:36 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Hans Reiser - Creator of ReiserFS
In-Reply-To: <1209655791.24465.2.camel@UbuntuGG>
References: <20080501034707.GA3713@xifos.org>
	<1209655791.24465.2.camel@UbuntuGG>
Message-ID: <1209659256.7462.12.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Thu, 2008-05-01 at 08:29 -0700, MJang wrote:
> On Wed, 2008-04-30 at 20:47 -0700, Luke Eckley wrote:
> > Hans Reiser, creator of the ReiserFS filesystem, has just been found
> > guilty of first degree murder.
> > 
> So is this the end of the ReiserFS, or are there any other developers
> who are willing to carry on that part of the codebase, perhaps with a
> name change?
> 
> Last time I tried OpenSUSE, the default FS was changed to ext3.

Reiserfs was pretty much abandoned years ago. Apparently, Novell has a
couple developers still doing maintenance because of SLES, but the code
is difficult to understand and no one wants to work on it.

Reiser4 is a different filesystem, the reason reiserfs was abandoned. It
was started in 2002 and ever since then Reiser has been unable to
convince Linus and friends to merge it. Because of the trial, Reiser's
company Namesys went out of business. Some of the Namesys developers
said they plan on continuing to work on reiser4 and perhaps even
creating a new company around it. Since the trial started, they have
made further effort to get reiser4 merged and some think it might make
it into the next kernel version. Only time will tell if reiser4 has a
future or if it doomed to become a dead end like reiserfs.

Hi there?

 Hope you are having a wonderful April. We are here at O'Reilly. We
just released some big news at our recent Web 2.0 Expo, our new
consulting and training division called O'Reilly InPractice. Here's an
interview with the new Chief Strategy Officer George LeBrun that's
worth a quick read.

 And as usual around here, we've got lots of new books, conferences,
and other projects going on. One of the latest is a new book widget
that lets you share O'Reilly and our partners' books on your web site.
You can choose by author, topic, or just show all our new releases
automatically and it's completely customizable for your web site so
you can choose the size, design, and more.

 I'm willing to trade you a book of your choice for posting one of
these cool new widgets. Send me a link and let us know how you like
them.

 Many thanks to our beta testers including Stephen Henry from NCMUG,
Michael Ross from SDOUG, Jen Blackledge from COWPU, and Satish Talim
from the RubyLearning User Group.

 New books we're hoping to get Amazon and Slashdot reviewers for
include "Web 2.0: A Strategy Guide," "The Principles of Project
Management," "Subject To Change," and "bash Cookbook." Send me an
email if your interested and I'll get the book right out.

 Travel Alert: Are you going to Tech?Ed or JavaOne? Please let me
know! I'll be attending both shows and want to say hi.

 We're also getting ready for OSCON in Portland, OR. If you have any
UG activities or ideas we should know about or attend--pass them
along. This will be our 10th Year of OSCON.

 And here's a funny video every cat person should watch--"An
Engineer's Guide to Cats"

I may have the program around here somewhere. I'll look.
The program was rather simple. Written in C or C++, it
used one function call to detect alternate streams within
a while loop that hit every file on the system; so, it
was slow. The anti-virus vendors I spoke to at the time
cited this performance issue as a reason why they did not
support ADS until it appeared in the wild.

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Sat May  3 15:49:18 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sat, 3 May 2008 15:49:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Alternate data streams...
In-Reply-To: <006c01c8a939$1a5d6ee0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <1208267773.4637.6.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<48156F42.3000905@alcpress.com>
	<006c01c8a939$1a5d6ee0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20080503224918.GD2379@sentinelchicken.org>

Hi Bill,

> Would you be aware of any modern day equivalents.

I don't know exactly what kind of interface you're looking for, but The
SleuthKit (TSK) should be able to easily obtain ADSs from NTFS.  The
only tricky part is interpreting the output.  NTFS considers all file
properties "attributes".  This includes file data.  A given object in
the filesystem may have multiple data attributes (all of those beyond
the first one are considered "alternate").  The NTFS data structures
even support assigning data attributes to directories, and directory
indexes to files.  (That's right, we're talking about file/dir gender
bending here.)

So as you can imagine, trying to build a single tool that can represent
all of this complexity while still supporting more traditional
filesystems would have been a pain.  Here's an example of TSK's output
using fls and istat (some output ommitted):

> fls /dev/sda1
r/r 7417-128-1: AUTOEXEC.BAT
r/r 4-128-4:    $AttrDef
r/r 8-128-2:    $BadClus
r/r 8-128-1:    $BadClus:$Bad
r/r 6-128-1:    $Bitmap
r/r 7-128-1:    $Boot
d/d 11-144-4:   $Extend
r/r 2-128-1:    $LogFile
r/r 0-128-1:    $MFT
r/r 1-128-1:    $MFTMirr
r/r 9-144-17:   $Secure:$SDH
r/r 9-144-16:   $Secure:$SII
r/r 9-128-0:    $Secure:$SDS
r/r 10-128-1:   $UpCase
r/r 3-128-3:    $Volume
d/d 19499-144-1:	ATI
r/r 3512-128-10:                boot.ini
r/r 7416-128-1:                 CONFIG.SYS
d/d 3520-144-6:                 Documents and Settings
r/r 7418-128-1:                 IO.SYS
r/r 7419-128-1:                 MSDOS.SYS
r/r 3483-128-3:                 NTDETECT.COM
r/r 3479-128-3:                 ntldr
r/r 23261-128-3:                pagefile.sys
d/d 3989-144-6:                 Program Files
d/d 10756-144-1:                RECYCLER
d/d 3518-144-6:                 System Volume Information
d/d 28-144-6:                   WINDOWS
d/d * 28-144-6(realloc):        WINDOWS
d/d * 28-144-6(realloc):        WINDOWS
d/d * 28-144-6(realloc):        WINDOWS
...


In the above output, the three numbers separated by '-' are important to
understand.  The first number of the three is kind of like a Unix
filesystem's inode number, in that it uniquely identifies the file.  The
second two numbers, I don't remember what they're for off hand, but they
can be used to uniquely identify a specific property of a file.

If you wanted more details about AUTOEXEC.BAT, you could use istat with
the MFT (inode) number:

> istat /dev/sda1 7417
MFT Entry Header Values:
Entry: 7417        Sequence: 1
$LogFile Sequence Number: 50333850
Allocated File
Links: 1

$STANDARD_INFORMATION Attribute Values:
Flags: Archive
Owner ID: 0
Created:  Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
File Modified:     Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
MFT Modified:      Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
Accessed:          Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007

$FILE_NAME Attribute Values:
Flags: Archive
Name: AUTOEXEC.BAT
Parent MFT Entry: 5 	Sequence: 5
Allocated Size: 0       Actual Size: 0
Created:  Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
File Modified:     Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
MFT Modified:      Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007
Accessed:          Sat Nov  3 14:54:25 2007

Attributes: 
Type: $STANDARD_INFORMATION (16-0)   Name: N/A   Resident   size: 72
Type: $FILE_NAME (48-2)   Name: N/A   Resident   size: 90
Type: $DATA (128-1)   Name: $Data   Resident   size: 0
...


Notice the $DATA attribute?  It's name is "$Data".  This is the default
data attribute for the file.  To get that attribute's contents, you can
append the second two numbers (128-1) to the MFT/inode number and run:

   icat /dev/sda1 7417-128-1


I don't have any ADSs right now, but I suspect if I did, they would be
numbered ...-128-2, ...-128-3, etc.

TSK has a lot of other tools to play with and is pretty easy to script
with.  I imagine you could write something to scour a filesystem quickly
for them.

good luck,
tim

have a range of issues with the 4965agn.

specifics of my problem, but before I do I need to check out everything
that wireless on Linux does. That is, I suspect it is a configuration
on my computer that is causing the problem. 

I need information about all utilities, GUI and otherwise. For example,
Network Manager used to show available networks about one time out of
20 tries. Wifi-Radar used to show all available networks every time.
>From the command line "sudo iwlist wlan0 scan" used to show all
available networks about three times out of four. Kwifimanager used to
show all available networks about one time out of four. (Right now,
none of them show any available networks ever, except the command line,
and it works only half the time.) And even when one of these displayed
all available networks I could usually not get an IP address.

l also need information about all configuration files and what they do,
how the syntax works, etc.

I'll never be able to get this thing working until I understand what
all the myriad pieces of wireless networking do, nor will I be able to

tell-the-whole-story-from-the-beginning sites. Can anyone suggest a

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Mon May  5 21:57:46 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Mon, 5 May 2008 21:57:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Desperate - Add CD-ROM to Hardy
In-Reply-To: <20080505213343.d4ad8ae2.johnxj@comcast.net>

	<00b201c8af30$2c98c6c0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

	<20080505213343.d4ad8ae2.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20080506045746.GI2276@sentinelchicken.org>

> Since the GUI thing doesn't work, I wonder if there is a command line way to install network-manager from the CD.
> 
> I guess it would be "sudo apt-get install <network-manager-whatever-it's-called>" but after cd to the CD-ROM drive


Taking a step back, can you just fix your network via the command line
(i.e. ifconfig and/or /etc/network/interfaces) and then install things
over the network again?  Wireless might be tricky if you're not used to
wpa_supplicant, but a wired connection shouldn't be hard to set up
temporarily for this.

dhclient eth0

  or

ifconfig eth0 <YOUR_IP> netmask 255.255.255.0
route add default gw <ROUTER_IP>
# finally edit your /etc/resolv.conf to set your DNS server

  or

# use your favorite editor to fix /etc/network/interfaces and
ifup eth0

  or what have you.

HTH,
tim

>>>>> "John" == John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> writes:

John> I can assure you that the ethernet port is not working. For
John> example, I have a Laserjet with a net connection set to
John> 192.168.0.15. If I turn it on and ping it, I get "Network is
John> unreachable." 

That just means that the interface isn't configured.  Check to see if
the interface is there:

  sudo ifconfig -a

do you see eth0?  If so, make sure you've got an ethernet cable to
your local network, and type:

  sudo dhclient eth0

If you've got a DHCP server on your network (you probably do), then
that should get you fixed up wired-network-wise.

If the ethernet interface is *not* present, then the module might have
been unloaded for some reason.  Try reloading it.

Network manager is just a gui interface.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

John,

Since you're using Ubuntu, you can go in and ensure the only source you
use for adding software is the CD-ROM.

Using bash:
$ sudo su -
# cd /etc/apt
# vi sources.list

(Comment out every line EXCEPT the first one:)
deb cdrom:(xxxxx)

On my system - Kubuntu Gutsy Gibbon, the first line actually reads:

deb cdrom:[Kubuntu 7.10 _Gutsy Gibbon_ - Release amd64 (20071016.1)]/
gutsy main restricted

Make sure you make a note of which lines you comment out as you'll want
to uncomment them later.

On my system I use:
#TJB
to comment each line.  That way I can readily identify lines I comment out vs those that should be commented out.

Good luck.

Tim

If the network card is there then follow the directions from others 
about configuring it.  If it's not there then try these steps...

$ lspci     # look for the ethernet controller
$ modprobe -l -t kernel/drivers/net   # look for a driver that matches 
your network card
$ sudo modprobe module   # where module is the network driver you found 
in step 2.

For the module name above, just use the name of the module, not the 
entire path, i.e. e1000 rather than kernel/drivers/net/e1000.ko.

> That just means that the interface isn't configured.  Check to see if
> the interface is there:
> 
>   sudo ifconfig -a
> 
> do you see eth0?  If so, make sure you've got an ethernet cable to
> your local network, and type:
> 
>   sudo dhclient eth0
> 
> If you've got a DHCP server on your network (you probably do), then
> that should get you fixed up wired-network-wise.
> 
> If the ethernet interface is *not* present, then the module might have
> been unloaded for some reason.  Try reloading it.
> 
> Network manager is just a gui interface.

and sure enough, the network was there. And "sudo dhclient eth0" got me
a connection. I'm back in business. I'm curious how I managed to kill
it, but that is not an important question. Thanks to all for your
patience.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue May  6 11:16:16 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 6 May 2008 11:16:16 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Change the locale on CentOS 4.3
In-Reply-To: <1210093963.7569.2.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
References: <1210093343.8683.1.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<1210093963.7569.2.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0805061107370.23108@guild.galois.com>

On Tue, 6 May 2008, Stuart Jansen wrote:

> On Tue, 2008-05-06 at 10:02 -0700, robinson-west user wrote:
>> The default seems to be en_US.UTF8 but mkisofs doesn't like that.
>> It does like en_US.cp437.  How do I change the default?  The locale
>> command only shows me the current settings.
>
> system-config-language
> or
> vi /etc/sysconfig/i18n

But don't do that. You don't want en_US.cp437 as your system language. 
Ungood things will result...

Read carefully the "CHARACTER SETS" section of the mkisofs(8) man page 
and adjust the -input-charset and -output-charset options as necessary 
-- or use "-input-charset help" to see what's built in. I'd suggest 
using iso8859-1 (or -15 for EU support) rather than cp437.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue May  6 13:39:14 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 6 May 2008 13:39:14 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Your favorite $SHELL verbs
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0805061328270.23108@guild.galois.com>

I saw this on Jesse Keating's blog (which he apparently lifted from a 
Planet Fedora discussion), a one-liner for discovering your favorite 
shell commands:

   history |\
   awk '{ a[$2]++ } END{ for( i in a ) { print a[i] " " i } }' |\
   sort -rn |\
   head

It won't take into account commands that are not the first token in 
any given line -- maybe they've been invoked by a wrapper like sudo or 
they sit on the other end of a pipe -- but it's still sort of 
interesting, in a navel-gazing sort of way.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue May  6 15:14:15 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 6 May 2008 15:14:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Your favorite $SHELL verbs
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0805061328270.23108@guild.galois.com>
References: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0805061328270.23108@guild.galois.com>
Message-ID: <200805061514.15737.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Paul Heinlein
# on Tuesday 06 May 2008:

>It won't take into account commands that are not the first token in
>any given line -- maybe they've been invoked by a wrapper like sudo or
>they sit on the other end of a pipe -- but it's still sort of
>interesting, in a navel-gazing sort of way.

It also only looks at the history of your current terminal (and I rarely 
have fewer than 20 of those.)  So, it might be more entertaining and/or 
informative to take a snapshot of currently running programs throughout 
the day if you really want to gaze all the way to the center of the 
navel.

  for i in /proc/{1..9}* ; do test -O $i \
   && basename $(readlink $i/exe) 2>/dev/null; done | sort -u

--Eric
-- 
So malloc calls a timeout and starts rummaging around the free chain,
sorting things out, and merging adjacent small free blocks into larger
blocks. This takes 3 1/2 days.
--Joel Spolsky

    http://scratchcomputing.com

The difference is that you have an IP address where I do not. But that is because I am at home where I do not use wireless.  If I was at PSU and was actually connected I would probably have a "131.x.x.x" in there. And my ethernet here shows an IP address where yours does not because that is how I am connected at the moment.

All the same except the following lines are different for me:

	Subsystem: Intel Corporation Lenovo ThinkPad T51
	Flags: bus master, fast devsel, latency 0, IRQ 504
	Memory at df2fe000 (64-bit, non-prefetchable) [size=8K]
	
> How does that compare to yours?

It appears that my wireless uses a different IRQ, has a different memory address, and yours doesn't know what the device is where mine is convinced it is a T51, even though it is really a T61. Sometimes computers get the silliest notions into their little silicon heads.

I doubt the IRQ and memory address are significant because mine is seen. An error in either of those should result in the device not even working, right?

I'm not sure any of the differences are significant.

Hmm well with that kind of spec, best thing would be a real featherweight
type distro, such as DSL. (http://damnsmalllinux.org/)

If you or someone you know is into Ham Radio, something like that would be a
perfect machine for contact logging, especially during Field Day.
http://www.arrl.org/contests/announcements/fd/
Basically Field Day is like a 24 hour camp out, contest, and emergency
preparedness exercise all in one.
Those that use laptops during FD for logging tend to use older ones they
wouldn't mind getting a little dirty or roughed up during a camp-out type
situation.

You could also use this old beast for serial console work (just a real basic
text only Linux install, with Minicom, to log into another machine by serial
port).

I have a slightly older Libretto -- 32 MB of RAM and a 133 MHz Pentium, 
but I think it's a 70 and not a 50. I managed to load DOS 5.0 from 
floppies on to the first 25 MB of the hard drive, then somehow got 
enough of Debian Woody on the hard drive to do an install. It wouldn't 
boot the Debian install floppies back then, but it might now. But it did 
come up and run Woody (early 2.4 or late 2.2 kernel, IIRC) and KDE 2 
something. Debian Potato was a slam dunk, and as someone else pointed 
out, you ought to be able to run Damn Small on it.

I'd still be using it if the battery was good, but it's sitting idle.

I have a Libretto 110 (Pentium 266) that would actually still be
perfectly serviceable if it weren't for the execrable hard drive
performance (no DMA for you!) and limited RAM (64 MB, max). It runs
Debian unstable just fine, although I haven't upgraded in a year or
so.

It was once pretty handy as a classroom PC, too (taking notes, reading
e-mail during boring lectures, etc.). Sadly, Firefox was all but
unusable on the Libretto. Opera was slightly better.  I've never
bothered with any fancy GUI desktop stuff. The ion window manager fits
the small display perfectly, and it's fast. For its final mission, I
stuck a 40 GB hard drive in it and use it in the garage as a poor
man's iPod.

On Tue, May 6, 2008 at 9:03 AM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
wrote:

> On 06 May 2008 01:45:24 -0700
> Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net> dijo:
>
> > >>>>> "John" == John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> writes:
> > John> I can assure you that the ethernet port is not working. For
> > John> example, I have a Laserjet with a net connection set to
> > John> 192.168.0.15. If I turn it on and ping it, I get "Network is
> > John> unreachable."
>
> > That just means that the interface isn't configured.  Check to see if
> > the interface is there:
> >
> >   sudo ifconfig -a
> >
> > do you see eth0?  If so, make sure you've got an ethernet cable to
> > your local network, and type:
> >
> >   sudo dhclient eth0
> >
> > If you've got a DHCP server on your network (you probably do), then
> > that should get you fixed up wired-network-wise.
> >
> > If the ethernet interface is *not* present, then the module might have
> > been unloaded for some reason.  Try reloading it.
> >
> > Network manager is just a gui interface.
>
> Tim already gave me that information but I was too tired last night to
> see what he was talking about. This morning I did "sudo ifconfig -a"
> and sure enough, the network was there. And "sudo dhclient eth0" got me
> a connection. I'm back in business. I'm curious how I managed to kill
> it, but that is not an important question. Thanks to all for your
> patience.

Hello and welcome to another edition of Know Your Distros!

RHEL/Fedora:   /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-*
SLES/openSUSE: /etc/sysconfig/network/ifcfg-*
Debian/Ubuntu: /etc/network/interfaces

>  > eek.  I think I'll let you keep that feature!

>  It's great being able to be able to search back through all of your old
>  commands, particularly when it's something from that window you just
>  closed..

This is possible in bash (assuming I understand correctly).  Below is
the relevant portion of my .bashrc -- all my history-related config is
here, it's not all needed for the shared history command "stuff":


# make a multi-line cmd into one line in history
shopt -s cmdhist

# don't put duplicate lines in the history. See bash(1) for more options
export HISTCONTROL=ignoredups
# ... and ignore same sucessive entries.
export HISTCONTROL=ignoreboth

# set up the prompt:
promptCommand() {
    # save the current history to disk on each command (this enables
on-the-fly history sharing between terminals)
    history -a

    ## Snipped a bunch of stuff to set a PS1 based on system status.
}
export PROMPT_COMMAND=promptCommand


--Rogan

That's easier than the answer I was going to give, which is to look at the
source file at
/usr/src/linux/drivers/net/wireless/iwlwifi/iwl4965-base.c:



/*
  * module name, copyright, version, etc.
  * NOTE: DRV_NAME is defined in iwlwifi.h for use by iwl-debug.h and printk
  */

#define DRV_DESCRIPTION "Intel(R) Wireless WiFi Link 4965AGN driver for Linux"

#ifdef CONFIG_IWLWIFI_DEBUG
#define VD "d"
#else
#define VD
#endif

#ifdef CONFIG_IWLWIFI_SPECTRUM_MEASUREMENT
#define VS "s"
#else
#define VS
#endif

#define IWLWIFI_VERSION "1.1.17k" VD VS
#define DRV_COPYRIGHT   "Copyright(c) 2003-2007 Intel Corporation"
#define DRV_VERSION     IWLWIFI_VERSION

/* Change firmware file name, using "-" and incrementing number,
  *   *only* when uCode interface or architecture changes so that it
  *   is not compatible with earlier drivers.
  * This number will also appear in << 8 position of 1st dword of uCode file */
#define IWL4965_UCODE_API "-1"


Carlos Konstanski

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Fri May  9 23:27:54 2008
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Fri, 9 May 2008 23:27:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] WAP11 1.14.1...
In-Reply-To: <1210397292.4372.1.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

> It is configured via 2000/XP/98 through a program that works over USB.

> Question is, is there a way to configure it from a Linux system?
>
> I was dismayed that this used AP didn't come with drivers, but I
> was able to download them at least.
>
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>

This is a Linksys WAP yes?
If so, it should have a web based configuration, like all Linksys routers.
It should be the default gateway address (such as 192.168.1.1, or
192.168.1.254 or etc), check the manual.
It should also have a standard default password, like username:admin,
pw:linksys, etc.  Again check manual.
>From there you can change settings to your liking.

What "drivers" do you need for a WAP?  You need drivers for a wireless card,
but the WAP should be OS agnostic, if your system supports 802.11a/b/g it
should work- as well as good ole 10/100 ethernet for direct interface and
admin.  The only "drivers" you might find would be the proprietary config
program, which you don't really need (shouldn't), and then possibly a
downloadable flash update for the firmware.

-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

I want to start backing up our remote servers using dirvish with SSH. 
This however requires that I allow remote root login through ssh.

I'm trying to figure out the best way of doing this.


In /etc/sshd_config I need to set
PermitRootLogin yes

But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key 
exchange and not allow remote password login?



Sean


From griffint at pobox.com  Sat May 10 13:37:20 2008
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Sat, 10 May 2008 13:37:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Best Practice for a Non-Best Practice task....
In-Reply-To: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
References: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200805101337.20502.griffint@pobox.com>

On Saturday 10 May 2008 12:43 pm, Sean Whitney wrote:
> I want to start backing up our remote servers using dirvish with SSH.
> This however requires that I allow remote root login through ssh.
>
> I'm trying to figure out the best way of doing this.
>
>
> In /etc/sshd_config I need to set
> PermitRootLogin yes
>
> But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key
> exchange and not allow remote password login?
>
>

I took a different approach but it requires some extra disk space on the
machine being backed up. I do a two-stage backup. In the first stage a cron
job running as root on the host being backed up, backs itself up in to tar
files in a dedicated local area for backups. Permissions for the tar files
are set such that user "backup" has read access. In stage two a cron job on a
remote host logs in via ssh using keys (rsync via ssh actually) as
user "backup" and mirrors the tar files. The backup user does not have a
password. You could also have the first host push the tar files to a
non-root account on another host, as long as the second host is not behind
a NAT'ing firewall or something.

Terry


From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Sat May 10 14:08:55 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sat, 10 May 2008 14:08:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Best Practice for a Non-Best Practice task....
In-Reply-To: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
References: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080510210855.GA15701@sentinelchicken.org>

> But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key 
> exchange and not allow remote password login?

Terry might have a better solution for you there, but I've done this
before for non-root users for system accounts and such.  You can simply
lock the user's password hash, disabling all password login for that
user with:

# password -l <USER>

However, ssh key logins should still work fine.  Of course for root that
means you can't su anymore, but you should be using sudo anyway, right?
Of course this solution may not work if you're using LDAP or something
as a backend (but who does that for root users anyway?).

HTH,
tim


From fredjame at fredjame.cnc.net  Sat May 10 14:44:58 2008
From: fredjame at fredjame.cnc.net (Fred James)
Date: Sat, 10 May 2008 16:44:58 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Best Practice for a Non-Best Practice task....
In-Reply-To: <200805101337.20502.griffint@pobox.com>
References: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
	<200805101337.20502.griffint@pobox.com>
Message-ID: <4826175A.8090304@fredjame.cnc.net>

Terry Griffin wrote:

>On Saturday 10 May 2008 12:43 pm, Sean Whitney wrote:
>  
>
>>I want to start backing up our remote servers using dirvish with SSH.
>>This however requires that I allow remote root login through ssh.
>>
>>I'm trying to figure out the best way of doing this.
>>
>>
>>In /etc/sshd_config I need to set
>>PermitRootLogin yes
>>
>>But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key
>>exchange and not allow remote password login?
>>
>>
>>    
>>
>
>I took a different approach but it requires some extra disk space on the
>machine being backed up. I do a two-stage backup. In the first stage a cron
>job running as root on the host being backed up, backs itself up in to tar
>files in a dedicated local area for backups. Permissions for the tar files
>are set such that user "backup" has read access. In stage two a cron job on a
>remote host logs in via ssh using keys (rsync via ssh actually) as
>user "backup" and mirrors the tar files. The backup user does not have a
>password. You could also have the first host push the tar files to a
>non-root account on another host, as long as the second host is not behind
>a NAT'ing firewall or something.
>
>Terry
>  
>
Out of necessity, I took it one step further (or to the side, if one 
prefers) ... I was doing the backup locally and then using a remote 
machine to pick up a copy, but for space constraints I had to gzip it on 
the go and the whole mess just took too long (running into the users and 
degrading the performance of both the machine and the database, plus 
making the backups iffy).

(short story)
Solution - move the backup process to the remote machine and gzip the 
files after the backups are copied completely.  I use a shell script 
(BASH, but Bourne would do as well) on A to get do the backups on B.  I 
embed expect scripts in the shell script to do whatever is needed on A 
(telnet, ssh, ftp), and B (sql included).  By redirecting the expect 
script like this ...
    /usr/bin/expect << EOF > $TempLogFile
... I capture an output file on A that my shell script can manipulate 
for required data to continue.

(long story)
... if you want more, just ask ... tell me what you need to know ... 
hope this helps
Regards
Fred James

On Sat, May 10, 2008 at 12:43 PM, Sean Whitney <sean.whitney at gmail.com> wrote:
> I want to start backing up our remote servers using dirvish with SSH.
> This however requires that I allow remote root login through ssh.
>
> I'm trying to figure out the best way of doing this.
>
>
> In /etc/sshd_config I need to set
> PermitRootLogin yes
>
> But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key
> exchange and not allow remote password login?

"man 5 sshd_config" says PermitRootLogin can be "without-password' in
which case password authentication is disabled for the root user. See
also the "forced-commands-only" option.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun May 11 12:35:34 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 11 May 2008 12:35:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Best Practice for a Non-Best Practice task....
In-Reply-To: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
References: <4825FAF8.9030708@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080511193534.GB25589@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sat, May 10, 2008 at 12:43:52PM -0700, Sean Whitney wrote:
> I want to start backing up our remote servers using dirvish with SSH. 
> This however requires that I allow remote root login through ssh.
> 
> I'm trying to figure out the best way of doing this.
> 
> In /etc/sshd_config I need to set
> PermitRootLogin yes
> 
> But is there a way to require root (an only root) to require ssh key 
> exchange and not allow remote password login?

SSH can be programmed to do this, see the sshd_config man page.  I
think you want "PermitRootLogin without-password", which permits 
keyed login but not password login;  not sure.

For extra security, you can do this through a VPN tunnel.  The double 
encryption adds a little runtime, but dirvish/rsync is efficient for
most data sets, and usually disk-access limited anyway.

Thw VPN/SSH setup will also facilitate remote system administration. 
Note that if you are backing up the whole machine, the backup client
has no secrets from the backup server anyway.

Keith

my boilerplate spam report. (The ISPs in Brittain frequently follow up with

didn't bother looking into it.

On Tue, May 13, 2008 at 10:04 AM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    I'm trying to write an awk/gawk script to alter the field sequence in a
>  data file containing about 300 lines. Because of different field separators
>  in each line I've not figure out how to write an expression that works.
>
>    The file is to insert data into a SQL database table. The original lines
>  are like this one:
>
>  INSERT INTO "Rules" VALUES('Few','Inorganics','Aquifer','GroundWater'
>
>    What I need for output is this:
>
>  INSERT INTO "Rules" VALUES('GroundWater','Aquifer','Inorganics','Few'
>
>  so I can finish the statements.

Can you use sqlite?

$ apt-get install sqlite

$ sqlite3 foo.db "create table Rules (a,b,c,d);"
$ cat <<eof | sqlite3 foo.db
INSERT INTO "Rules" VALUES('Few','Inorganics','Aquifer','GroundWater');
eof
$ sqlite3 foo.db "alter table Rules rename to bar;"
$ sqlite3 foo.db "create table Rules as select d,c,b,a from bar;"
$ sqlite3 foo.db ".dump Rules"
BEGIN TRANSACTION;
CREATE TABLE 'Rules'(d,c,b,a);
INSERT INTO "Rules" VALUES('GroundWater','Aquifer','Inorganics','Few');
COMMIT;

Pipe that through grep to get just the inserts.

Regards,
- Robert

command.

[get data] | sed "s/ /,/" | awk [print your stuff in the order you want]

> BEGIN {FS = " "}
>       {print $1, $2, $3}
>       {FS = ","}
>       {print $4, "," $3, "," $2, "," $1}

> --
> Richard B. Shepard, Ph.D.
> |  Integrity            Credibility
> Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc.        |            Innovation
> <http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517      Fax:
> 503-667-8863


 I'm sort of simplistic when it comes to this stuff.

I would pipe the output to sed, then pipe the output of that to your awk
command.

[get data] | sed "s/ /,/" | awk [print your stuff in the order you want]

-wes


From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Tue May 13 13:40:02 2008
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Tue, 13 May 2008 13:40:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Debian OpenSSL predictable keys
Message-ID: <c29537840805131340y6cace6a5ve94fa32d113c9fb2@mail.gmail.com>

This is pretty bad if you're running Debian or any derivative (e.g. Ubuntu):

http://lists.debian.org/debian-security-announce/2008/msg00152.html

This also affects anyone who migrated keys generated on a Debian
system to any other system.  The OpenSSL guys aren't very happy with
Debian right now:

http://www.links.org/?p=327

Jason

On Tue, May 13, 2008 at 3:40 PM, Jason Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> wrote:
> This is pretty bad if you're running Debian or any derivative (e.g. Ubuntu):

Ubuntu already has the fix in the repositories:

$ sudo apt-get update && sudo apt-get upgrade

which will also resets your hosts keys.

Debian also has the fix, but you have to reset the host keys yourself.

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Wed May 14 03:25:57 2008
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Wed, 14 May 2008 03:25:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] openssh-server upgrade/problem resolved
In-Reply-To: <482A8826.8030305@verizon.net>
References: <482A0085.9030402@verizon.net> <482A8826.8030305@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <da2207740805140325g308cde64x900ed11aed46ebe@mail.gmail.com>

Did you hear about the SSH vulnerability that was patched in Debian and all
it's derivatives (including Ubuntu)?
Debian:
http://lists.debian.org/debian-security-announce/2008/msg00152.html

Ubuntu
https://lists.ubuntu.com/archives/ubuntu-security-announce/2008-May/000705.html

Note this was a Debian (and derivative) specific issue, with the packages
(random number generator wasn't really random, getting "dirty" data), not an
issue with OpenSSH or OpenSSL itself.

This is most likely the reason.
Not sure about the templates issue you saw but it could be there was
something related that was also fixed...

As a side note you may want to regenerate your SSH keys on your Ubuntu box,
if you log into it remotely (probably even if you don't).

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Wed May 14 13:05:11 2008
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)
Date: Wed, 14 May 2008 13:05:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] openssh-server upgrade/problem resolved
In-Reply-To: <482B1418.5030605@verizon.net>
References: <482A0085.9030402@verizon.net>
	<482A8826.8030305@verizon.net>	<da2207740805140325g308cde64x900ed11aed46ebe@mail.gmail.com>
	<482B1418.5030605@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <482B45F7.9010805@gmail.com>

Upgrading your ubuntu boxes will result in regenerating the hostkey.

On debian boxes check the date of the keys, if they are prior to 
September 2006, they are probably ok, otherwise I think you need to 
replace the keys yourself.

ssh-keygen -f /etc/ssh/ssh_host_rsa_key -t rsa -N ''
ssh-keygen -f /etc/ssh/ssh_host_dsa_key -t dsa -N ''

Also there is a new package available in ubuntu called 
openssh-blacklist.  It's also available in and in lenny and etch, using

deb http://security.debian.org/debian-security etch/updates main

Installing this will prevent compromised keys from logging into boxes.

Also check out the new command ssh-vulnkey in the openssh-client 
package.  It uses the openssh-blacklist package to highlight compromised 
keys.

Finally ssh-keygen -lf <file> will help you generate fingerprints to 
help located bad keys.



Sean




> Matt McKenzie wrote:
>> Did you hear about the SSH vulnerability that was patched in Debian and all
>> it's derivatives (including Ubuntu)?
>> Debian:
>> http://lists.debian.org/debian-security-announce/2008/msg00152.html

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Wed May 14 14:15:31 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Wed, 14 May 2008 14:15:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Debian OpenSSL predictable keys
In-Reply-To: <c29537840805131340y6cace6a5ve94fa32d113c9fb2@mail.gmail.com>
References: <c29537840805131340y6cace6a5ve94fa32d113c9fb2@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080514211531.GD2515@sentinelchicken.org>

> This is pretty bad if you're running Debian or any derivative (e.g. Ubuntu):

If you still doubt how bad this is, see:
  http://metasploit.com/users/hdm/tools/debian-openssl/

To exploit weak SSH host keys, an attacker would likely need to
man-in-the-middle the connection.  To exploit SSH client keys, he would
merely need to know/guess a user name that has a weak key and then grid
at it for a while in an online attack.  Similar attacks would exist for
SSL and any other keys generated by openssl under debian, et. al.

tim

Michael Ewan wrote:
> Or you could use a better file system like xfs or reiserfs.  Ext2/3 is 
> SLOW and even with journaling on ext3 you still need to fsck periodicaly 
> like you describe.  XFS and Reiserfs are Btree based and have a native 
> integrated journal so unless your file system has major damage, they 
> won't need to fsck, ever.

Both XFS and ReiserFS (and JFS as well) have some quirks, some 
situations where they rock and some situations where they suck. I've 
seen lots of filesystem benchmarks go by and my net conclusion was that, 
unless you're doing the exact kind of I/O that one of the "alternative" 
filesystems rocks with, you're better off accepting the default you 
distro installs. Most of them default to ext3, but I think one of them 
does default to reiserfs 3.

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Thu May 15 07:19:00 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Thu, 15 May 2008 08:19:00 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu 30-boot forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <482C3B80.7000009@comcast.net>
References: <20080514224046.44115b1d.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<1210830963.10724.5.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
	<482C3B80.7000009@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1210861140.10724.26.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Thu, 2008-05-15 at 06:32 -0700, Michael Ewan wrote:
> Or you could use a better file system like xfs or reiserfs.  Ext2/3 is 
> SLOW and even with journaling on ext3 you still need to fsck periodicaly 
> like you describe.  XFS and Reiserfs are Btree based and have a native 
> integrated journal so unless your file system has major damage, they 
> won't need to fsck, ever.

Actually, most filesystems are really good at certain things and pretty
sucky at others. ext2/3 on the other hand tends to be middle of the
road, rarely the best and never the worst. Because it is the official
Linux filesystem, it tends to have a larger number of developers
maintaining it and better integration with other Linux features. For
example, ext3 and reiserfs can grow and shrink whereas xfs and jfs can
only grow. ext3 and xfs can store selinux labels, but reiserfs and jfs
can't. ext3 is supported by all distros, some don't even include
reiserfs, xfs or jfs and of those that do include them many don't
officially support them.

My personal approach is to use ext3 for most filesystems, and XFS when I
want its unique advantages. I have a couple of XFS filesystems
under /data because compared to ext3, I'm able to store about 10% more
data on XFS. I tend to avoid resierfs these days. And JFS isn't
available on some of the distros I use.



From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu May 15 07:48:06 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 15 May 2008 07:48:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu 30-boot forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <20080514224046.44115b1d.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080514224046.44115b1d.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20080515144806.GB13857@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, May 14, 2008 at 10:40:46PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I've been bitching about this for years. Every 30th boot Ubuntu tells
> me it needs to do an fsck. I think that is a great idea, except it
> always pops up when I am in a professr's office and have just said "let
> me show you what I mean ..." It never happens when I have a lot of time
> and it doesn't matter. A "next time, please" button is what I have
> begged for.

It would be nice if there was advanced warning - that is, something
that comes up and says "After 4 more reboots, I'm going to do an 
auto-fsck".  You really ought to do this more often than the value
written with tune2fs.  I suggest looking around for some program that
can provide this warning, say sending you an email when an fsck is
imminent.  There is probably such a program out there;  if not,
somebody could write a script that takes apart the information from:

   sudo /sbin/dumpe2fs -h /dev/hdaXXX 

... where XXX is one of your partitions.  dumpe2fs will tell you (among
many other things) things like:

   ...
   Mount count:              10
   Maximum mount count:      33
   Last checked:             Mon Mar 17 09:11:16 2008
   Check interval:           15552000 (6 months)
   Next check after:         Sat Sep 13 09:11:16 2008
   ...

Given an imminent fsck, you can force a premature fsck reboot when
there is time available by doing a "sudo touch /forcefsck" followed
by a reboot from the menu, or a "sudo /sbin/shutdown -F -r" which
does the fsck-forcing reboot.  When that is done, it will reset
the counters to 33 or whatever is set by tune2fs.

You really ought to do an fsck every 3 months or so.  Hard drives do
unravel at the edges over time, and fsck cleans up some of the damage. 
In my case, I leave my laptop up all the time (suspending when I
transport it) and I am surprised by the "fsck after 180 days"
limit more often than I run into the maximum mount count limit.  Ditto
my servers, which only go down for kernel upgrades.

Running a file system like reiserfs just moves the "unravelling" problem
deeper down.  Yes, it will recover more often than ext3, but when it
encounters a problem it can't handle (and there are *always* classes of
problems unanticipated by the author) you are pretty much hosed.  I don't
know about the reliability/fixability of XFS or other advanced file
systems, they may be better, but nothing is perfect, and no advanced file
system written in (say) 2006 is going to accomodate all the weird new
failure mechanisms that an advanced technology drive released in 2007
or later may produce.  Worst case, the drive manufacturers may be
incorporating hardware level fixes optimized for windoze NTFS that
totally hose the repair mechanisms for the Linux file system.  In that
case, you want to use a popular file system that is (1) supported by your
distro, and (2) frequently upgraded by your distro supplier, so that
there is a chance that these hardware quirks get noticed and accomodated.

At the end of the day, be thankful that these inconvenient fscks happen
*sometime*.  Windoze ignores most of these problems, and your data is at
much higher risk.  The bits on a modern hard drive are tiny and fragile,
and you lose some from time to time, even if the disk is just sitting
there unused.  The hardware makes corrections, but not 100% perfectly,
and rarely-visited files aren't frequently tested for these bit
failures.  fsck examines the entire partition, forcing a hardware-level
rewrite if nothing else, and keeping the system healthy.  That means
that you will be faced with catastrophic failures much less often.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From creswick at gmail.com  Thu May 15 10:16:14 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Thu, 15 May 2008 10:16:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu 30-boot forced fsck
In-Reply-To: <482C6B89.1020505@dslnorthwest.net>
References: <20080514224046.44115b1d.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20080515144806.GB13857@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<482C6B89.1020505@dslnorthwest.net>
Message-ID: <c834ec80805151016k61a8d461h53030a98f6508651@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/15/08, Denis Heidtmann <denish at dslnorthwest.net> wrote:
>  Partition /dev/hdb1 is mounted with write permissions, cannot check it
>
>  How to handle this? There was something I read about single-user mode, but it
>  was beyond my knowledge.  Is a live CD boot an option?  It seems to me that a
>  read-only test limits the type of problems that can be detected.

You generally can't check a filesystem that can be written to because
something might change while the check is going on, and it could
incorrectly report errors.  There may very well be more dangerous
consequences too -- I'm not sure, but it seems reasonable that the FS
check is going to be doing things that normal programs shouldn't
interfere with.

Therefore, you've got to mount the partition as read only:

$ sudo mount -o remount,ro /dev/hdb1

should do it.

When the check is done:

$ sudo mount -o remount,rw /dev/hdb1

(running from memory, the last part may need to be a mount point, eg:
/ not a device)

Note that you probably can't write the check log to /dev/hdb1 if
you're checking /dev/hdb1...

--Rogan

	<c834ec80805151016k61a8d461h53030a98f6508651@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620805151056x256f064aj9bcd68b490a514a8@mail.gmail.com>

On 5/15/08, Rogan Creswick <creswick at gmail.com> wrote:
> On 5/15/08, Denis Heidtmann <denish at dslnorthwest.net> wrote:
>  >  Partition /dev/hdb1 is mounted with write permissions, cannot check it
>  >
>  >  How to handle this? There was something I read about single-user mode, but it
>  >  was beyond my knowledge.  Is a live CD boot an option?  It seems to me that a
>  >  read-only test limits the type of problems that can be detected.
>
>
> You generally can't check a filesystem that can be written to because
>  something might change while the check is going on, and it could
>  incorrectly report errors.  There may very well be more dangerous
>  consequences too -- I'm not sure, but it seems reasonable that the FS
>  check is going to be doing things that normal programs shouldn't
>  interfere with.
>
>  Therefore, you've got to mount the partition as read only:
>
>  $ sudo mount -o remount,ro /dev/hdb1
>
>  should do it.
>
>  When the check is done:
>
>  $ sudo mount -o remount,rw /dev/hdb1
>
>  (running from memory, the last part may need to be a mount point, eg:
>  / not a device)

it works either way (device or mount point).  you may also find the
"-n" flag to mount useful--i use it by reflex now, since it was at
some point required to move an FS from R/O to R/W or back.

note:  mounting an actively in-use (ie being written to) volume
read-only may cause some havoc with stuff crashing as it can't write
needed files.  gnome definitely tanked once when i did that.  it's
been years since i used gnome though, so hopefully developers have
gotten smarter with failing gracefully...  and certainly you'll lose
some syslog info if you were to do that to the partition syslog would
write to.

so don't leave it r/o for longer than you need =)

This ability to escape out of  the maintenance e2fsck  at boot was added in
an Ubuntu patch to the initscripts.

http://patches.ubuntu.com/by-release/atomic/ubuntu/s/sysvinit/sysvinit_2.86.ds1-14.1ubuntu35.patch

You might be able  to apply this patch on your laptop, copy over the patched
script, or upgrade sysvinit.

Bill Barry

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> Has anyone built a graph or chart showing ping times between various
> networks in the Portland area?  Are there internet providers that
> tend to have better local connectivity?  It would be nice to know
> which network to put a local server on, for example, or keep track
> of network improvements.

Here is one place to start:

http://www.nwax.net/


I helped on the technical side of this when I was with PSU.  The theory is the
members that are located at NWAX will exchange local BGP routes, thus keeping as
much traffic off their expensive tier-1 upstreams as possible.

It was up to the members to setup those agreements between each other, so you
have a partial mesh of peering agreements.  Some members allocate more time to
that than others.

It's nice I can get to mirrors.cat.pdx.edu from my Easystreet colo through NWAX.

krisa at cylon(~)$ traceroute mirrors.cat.pdx.edu
traceroute to mirrors.cat.pdx.edu (131.252.208.96), 30 hops max, 40 byte packets

 1.290 ms

From qhartman at gmail.com  Fri May 16 12:04:13 2008
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Fri, 16 May 2008 13:04:13 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Convert .flv to something
In-Reply-To: <20080516114442.245c270c.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080516114442.245c270c.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0805161204t21bba5a0t1f764825cdfb78e7@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, May 16, 2008 at 12:44 PM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
wrote:

> What I need to know most is what format would be best for playing the
> video when placed in an OOo Impress file running on Windows. I know
> nothing whatsoever about movie formats, so any suggestions are welcome.
>

ffmpeg to convert to an MPG file is probably your best, following that maybe
mplayer/mencoder, or avidemux (though I'm not sure avidemux supports flv).
ffmpeg is incredibly featureful and generally can do a good job, but the
defaults are often "sub-optimal". So, if the quality is noticeably degraded
from the original start poking at the options and see if you can improve
things by explicitly specifying various options.
http://howto-pages.org/ffmpeg/#basicvideo is a reasonable starting place,
though I have no idea if this is more or less sophisticated than what you've
already done.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

When that happens to me, I add an "su" to the crontab line ... something 
like this ...
    0 5 * * * su - username -c command
... another thing that I find helps in some cases is to give the full 
path name of the built-ins
    /bin/echo instead of echo, for example
Mostly that has been on SGI IRIX, but occasionally on Linux
Hope that helps

From dpape at dpape.com  Fri May 16 22:08:02 2008
From: dpape at dpape.com (Daniel Pape)
Date: Fri, 16 May 2008 22:08:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] cdrecord from cronjob
In-Reply-To: <482E58DB.3000706@acm.org>
References: <482E58DB.3000706@acm.org>
Message-ID: <482E6832.8000909@dpape.com>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> 
> Since it works from the command line I'm assuming I've got all the 
> pieces write but it pukes from cron.

I've seen things like this happen a few times. I attribute it to the 
fact that cron usually runs commands with SHELL set to /bin/sh, which is 
  usually (but not always?) different than /bin/bash.

To fix this I often just make a small script to contain the commands I 
want cron to run. Then just make sure that script has #!/bin/bash (or 
whatever you need) at the top. cron will then run that script which can 
have the normal environment you expect on the command line.


dan

From mcintoshrt at gmail.com  Fri May 16 23:28:31 2008
From: mcintoshrt at gmail.com (Robert McIntosh)
Date: Fri, 16 May 2008 23:28:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] cdrecord from cronjob
In-Reply-To: <482E58DB.3000706@acm.org>
References: <482E58DB.3000706@acm.org>
Message-ID: <482E7B0F.2080305@gmail.com>

If it's not a path problem, could you bump up the error reporting level 
and post what's going on?

I used to run cdrecord religiously from cron usually without incident 
(when the scsi trickery didn't shuffle the dev entry).  Something like 
this worked well as encrypted back-ups with some error checking that 
sends a success/fail to the administrator:

===
#!/bin/sh

TODAY=`(set \`date\`;echo $1)`
ISOMAXSIZE=665600
mkdir /var/cdtemp > /dev/null 2>&1
CDTEMPDIR=/var/cdtemp
rm -f -R /var/cdtemp
mkdir /var/cdtemp
chown root::root /var/cdtemp
chmod 700 /var/cdtemp

#back-up databases
SOURCE=/var/lib/mysql
USERNAME=yourusername
PASSWORD=yourpassword
OUTPUT=/var/dbbackups
LOGFILE=/var/log/mysqllogback.txt
DATABASES=`mysql -B -u $USERNAME --password=$PASSWORD -e 'show 
databases' | grep -v Database`
 for DB in $DATABASES ; do
    EMPTYDB=`mysql -B -u $USERNAME --password=$PASSWORD -e 'show tables' 
$DB | wc -l`
    if [ $EMPTYDB -ge 1 ] ; then
        echo `date`": Starting backup of database $DB" 2>>$LOGFILE 
1>>$LOGFILE
        ( time /usr/bin/mysqlhotcopy $DB --allowold -u $USERNAME -p 
$PASSWORD $OUTPUT ) 2>>$LOGFILE 1>>$LOGFILE
        [ $? != 0 ] && echo "mysqlhotcopy of database $DB failed on 
"`hostname -f`"."
        echo `date`": Finished backup of database $DB" 2>>$LOGFILE 
1>>$LOGFILE
    else
       echo `date`": Database $DB empty - no backup is done" 2>>$LOGFILE 
1>>$LOGFILE
    fi
 done
 
 unset USERNAME
 unset PASSWORD
 unset OUTPUT
#First creat the archives
#echo "Creating /root archive"
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/root.tar.gz /root > /dev/null 2>&1
#echo "Creating /home archive"
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/robertcron.tar.gz /home/robert/cron > /dev/null 2>&1
#
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/etc.tar.gz /etc > /dev/null 2>&1
#echo "Creating cron back-ups"
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/cron.tar.gz /var/cron > /dev/null 2>&1
#echo "Creating web server back-ups"
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/www.tar.gz /var/www > /dev/null 2>&1
#echo "Creating mysql back-ups
tar cfz $CDTEMPDIR/mysql.tar.gz /var/dbbackups > /dev/null 2>&1

/bin/echo yourpasswordhere | /usr/bin/gpg --no-tty -c --yes 
--passphrase-fd 0 $CDTEMPDIR/root.tar.gz > /dev/null 2>&1
/bin/echo yourpasswordhere | /usr/bin/gpg --no-tty -c --yes 
--passphrase-fd 0 $CDTEMPDIR/etc.tar.gz > /dev/null 2>&1
/bin/echo yourpasswordhere | /usr/bin/gpg --no-tty -c --yes 
--passphrase-fd 0 $CDTEMPDIR/cron.tar.gz > /dev/null 2>&1
/bin/echo yourpasswordhere | /usr/bin/gpg --no-tty -c --yes 
--passphrase-fd 0 $CDTEMPDIR/www.tar.gz > /dev/null 2>&1
/bin/echo yourpasswordhere | /usr/bin/gpg --no-tty -c --yes 
--passphrase-fd 0 $CDTEMPDIR/mysql.tar.gz > /dev/null 2>&1
#Create the ISO Image of the archives
#/bin/echo "Creating iso image"
/usr/bin/mkisofs -o $CDTEMPDIR/backup.iso -l -J -R -A -V 
$CDTEMPDIR/*.tar.gz.gpg > /dev/null 2>&1

BACKUPISOSIZE=`du $CDTEMPDIR/backup.iso | cut -f 1`
BURNEDSIZE=`du /mnt/dvd | cut -f 1`
if [ "$BACKUPISOSIZE" -gt "$ISOMAXSIZE" ]
then
echo "ISO filesize too large to burn to CD-RW." > /root/cron/toobigiso
mail -s "Back-Up Image Too Large!" root < /root/cron/toobigiso
else
#Burn the ISO Image to CD-RW
#echo "Burning CD"

###!!!Find out what your --dev entry should be with cdrecord --dev-help
/usr/bin/cdrecord --dev=/dev/cdrom blank=fast > /var/log/burncd.log 2>&1
sleep 10
/usr/bin/cdrecord --dev=/dev/cdrom speed=16 $CDTEMPDIR/backup.iso >> 
/var/log/burncd.log 2>&1
mount /dev/hda /mnt/dvd >> /var/log/burncd.log 2>&1
umount -f /mnt/dvd

#Search the log for error messages
#grep -i warning /var/log/burncd.log >> /var/log/burncderror.log
grep -i error /var/log/burncd.log >> /var/log/burncderror.log
grep -i busy /var/log/burncd.log >> /var/log/burncderror.log
grep -i notice /var/log/burncd.log >> /var/log/burncderror.log
grep -i timeout /var/log/burncd.log >> /var/log/burncderror.log

#If we find errors, report them to the specified back-up administrator"
if test -s /var/log/burncderror.log
then
/bin/echo "$HOSTNAME generated errors during the back-up." 
 >>/var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "The back-up may not have completed successfully." >> 
/var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "Please review the following error messages:" >> /var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "" >> /var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "-----" >> /var/log/bcderror
cat /var/log/burncderror.log >> /var/log/bcderror
mail -s "$HOSTNAME Back-Up Error" root < /var/log/bcderror
rm -f /var/log/burncd.log
rm -f /var/log/bcderror.log
rm -f /var/log/bcderror
rm -f /var/log/burncderror.log
rm -f /var/ftp/pub/backup/*.*
rm -f /var/ftp/pub/backup/*.*.*
rm -f /var/ftp/pub/backup.iso

mount /mnt/dvd
if [$BURNEDSIZE = 0]
then
/bin/echo "$HOSTNAME generated errors during the bak-up." >> 
/var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "The back-up may not have completed succesfully." >> 
/var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "Please review the disc for errors."  >> /var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "" >> /var/log/bcderror
/bin/echo "-----" >>/var/log/bcderror
mail -s "$HOSTNAME Back-Up Error" root < /var/log/bcderror
rm -f /var/log/burncd.log
rm -f /var/log/bcderror.log
rm -f /var/log/bcderror
rm -f /var/log/burncderror.log
rm -f $CDTEMPDIR/*
fi


#E-mail the administrator 'Success!'
/bin/echo "Back-up for $TODAY completed successfully." >> /root/jobdone
echo "" >> /root/jobdone
echo "The following directories were backed-up:" >> /root/jobdone
echo "/etc (system configuration files)" >> /root/jobdone
echo "/usr/local/etc (server application file configuration" >> 
/root/jobdone
echo "/root (System Adminstration files)" >> /root/jobdone
echo "/usr/local/www (Website)" >> /root/jobdone
echo "The size of the back-up volume is:" >> /root/jobdone
mount /dev/hdc /media/dvd > /dev/null 2>&1
du -h /media/dvd | cut -f 1 >> /root/jobdone
umount -f /media/dvd
echo "When the size is about 650 MB, you'll need to trim some files 
or">>/root/jobdone
echo "new backup solution." >> /root/jobdone  
mail -s "Backup Completed Succesfully" root < /root/jobdone
#rm /root/jobdone
rm -f -R /var/log/bcderror.log/
#rm -f /var/log/burncd.log
fi
fi
rm -f $CDTEMPDIR/*

I have found that most strange text in man pages can be rendered 
properly by doing
export LC_ALL=C
in your shell.  As stated above, it's a local problem.  As side effect 
of LC_ALL=C is that ls will sort ascii, so case and dot files sort as 
we're used to.

On Mon, 19 May 2008, Michael Ewan wrote:

> Paul Mullen wrote:
>> On Sun, May 18, 2008 at 11:54:35AM -0700, Russell Senior wrote:
>>
>>> I noticed a while back that manpages on one of my (debian) desktop 
>>> boxes have mangled rendering due to uninterpretted terminal 
>>> control codes in an xterm, e.g. (randomly) from the jhead manpage:
>>>
>>
>> Mine does the same thing. It seems to have something to do with 
>> character sets and locales. I have $LANG set to "en_US.UTF-8". 
>> Setting it to "C" produces readable man pages.
>>
>>
> I have found that most strange text in man pages can be rendered 
> properly by doing export LC_ALL=C in your shell.  As stated above, 
> it's a local problem.  As side effect of LC_ALL=C is that ls will 
> sort ascii, so case and dot files sort as we're used to.

LC_COLLATE is the variable that determines sort order. I set it to "C" 
in all shells to get the traditional ls listings. (LC_ALL is the trump 
card of locale variables, overriding LANG and LC_*.)

My assessment is that you'd be better off fixing your UTF-8 display 
issues than ignoring them by forcing everything to ASCII.

Does locale(1) show that you have en_US.utf8 stuff installed?

   locale -a | grep ^en_US

On Debian systems, /etc/locale.gen determines what locales get built 
when libc is updated.

Debian also includes a validlocale(8) utility that can be handy. On 
this system, I've got American English Unicode installed, but not 
native German (my forebears would be aghast!):

   [heinlein at monk ~]$ validlocale en_US.UTF-8
   locale 'en_US.UTF-8' valid and available
   [heinlein at monk ~]$ validlocale de_DE.UTF-8
   locale 'de_DE.UTF-8' not available
   de_DE.UTF-8 UTF-8

The very last line -- "de_DE.UTF-8 UTF-8" -- is what I would add to 
/etc/locale.gen if I wanted to make German available. Then I'd run 
locale-gen(8) to bootstrap the new locale.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

I've been too lazy, up until now. "validlocale" showed that

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon May 19 15:34:30 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Mon, 19 May 2008 15:34:30 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] email joe-job.
In-Reply-To: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
References: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
Message-ID: <48091.75.164.221.130.1211236470.squirrel@clueserver.org>

> Looks like I've had a joe-job done to my main email address.  Forged
> envelopes with my address as the Return-Path.  A couple of thousand
> bounce messages since around 10:30 this morning and still coming in.

It is not a joejob..  It is spam with your address as the return address.

A JoeJob is when someone sends out mail to nail you personally.

This is just a case of someone picking a random address off the net and
setting it as a return address.  (It happens all the time.  I have gotten
these for at least the last 10 years off and on.)

Examples of joe jobs are like the mail I got a while back claiming that
you could buy drugs and/or kid porn films at a site owned by a group of
Russian Jews.  It was done to cause law enforcement and/or vigilante
action against the site.  That is not happening in your case.


From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon May 19 15:44:42 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 19 May 2008 15:44:42 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] email joe-job.
In-Reply-To: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
References: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.10.0805191539130.63573@guild.galois.com>

On Mon, 19 May 2008, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> Looks like I've had a joe-job done to my main email address. 
> Forged envelopes with my address as the Return-Path.  A couple of 
> thousand bounce messages since around 10:30 this morning and still 
> coming in. [....]

Bummer.

I can't believe that there are still that many mail servers that are 
queueing mail without running spam/virus filters during the SMTP 
transaction -- in which case the sending MTA is responsible for the 
bounce. When the sender is a spambot, which is likely in this case, 
the failure notice is ignored, because the $$$ are made by sending 
spam, not forged bounces.

Spam's been a major problem for years now. Why are recipient MTAs 
still accepting responsibility for bouncing spam?

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

I can think of a number of reasons.  Most people who run mail servers do
not have any clue how to configure them correctly.  Or they picked
software that does not filter well.  Or they use packages that do stupid
things like generate bounce messages on viruses and other bogus content.

There are over 1,000 solutions to spam.  Most of them are bad.

out for order tracking to a company I've never used before.  But when it 
went wild with thousands I got worried my system or account might have 
been compromised.  It hasn't!

So I guess I'm in it for a bit longer.


Rod

From griffint at pobox.com  Mon May 19 17:23:39 2008
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Mon, 19 May 2008 17:23:39 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] email joe-job.
In-Reply-To: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
References: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org>
Message-ID: <20493.198.102.182.114.1211243019.squirrel@webmail.whiz.to>

> Looks like I've had a joe-job done to my main email address.  Forged
> envelopes with my address as the Return-Path.  A couple of thousand
> bounce messages since around 10:30 this morning and still coming in.
>
> Any ideas as to how to identify that they are bounce messages.  Is there
> a postfix header check or a procmail recipe?
>
> I'll probably start with the Sender containing postmaster and dump that
> into a folder.  That should cut down on the pile I have to sort through
> so I don't miss a real message.
>
> Not sure Spam Assassin would help and this is a pretty old mail server,
> running postfix, that has never been compromised before, so installing
> Spam Assassin isn't a good option.  I'm working towards replacing it but
> not ready yet.  Looks like I might have to get ready sooner.
>

The term is "backscatter" when you are not being deliberately targeted
but instead your email address is simply being abused.

Dealing with backscatter has been my struggle for the past many weeks,
but I've more or less got it under control now with some custom filters
that I slapped together.

First I try to determine if the message is some sort of bounce, legit
or not. If so then I try to find the original message either as an
attachment or in-line. In-line is most difficult because there are so
many different formats with different delimiters. But if I can find the
original message then I run it through the regular spam filters. If it
turns up positive then I assume my bounce is backscatter.

If I can't find the original message then I just search the body of the
bounce message for the original "From:" line. If I find it I pick out the
name and address. Say for example it's this:

   From: "go large" <myaddress at example.com>

(I also check the "From:" line even in the cases where I do find the
entire attached message, but where it passes the regular spam filters.)

The backscatter clue is that the string "go large" does not match
the string "Terry Griffin". [Insert your own joke here.] For myself and
every user on my mail server I know their real name. I lookup the name
using the email address from the "From:" line, then do a fuzzy string
comparison between their name and whatever name is in the "From:" line.
If there is no similarity then I assume the original message was spam
with a forged address, and that the bounce is backscatter.

Some bounces unfortunately don't contain enough of the original spam
to work with. But even so I'm now getting a backscatter catch rate
well in to the 90% range and no false positives.

Terry

On Mon, 19 May 2008, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> Thanks all.  Alan you are correct.  I jumped too deep into it.  The fact
> it looks like I'm sending all these messages out made it feel like a joe
> job.  I run a pretty tight mail server and double checked to make sure it
> wasn't an open relay of any flavor.

Rod,

   In my postfix header checks I have a line that looks like this:

/^Received: from nnn.nn.nnn.nn/                 reject Rule H9

where the obfuscated part is my public IP address. Since my MTA will never
receive mail from the outward-looking IP address I know it has to have been
spoofed. Catches quite a few every day.

   Don't know if that will help you or not, but I offer it in case it's
something you've not yet implemented.

Rich

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon May 19 18:28:08 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 19 May 2008 18:28:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Thinkpad, nVidia, External Monitor, and PSU
Message-ID: <20080519182808.bab5db27.johnxj@comcast.net>

I need to do a class presentation at PSU. OOo Impress does a dandy job
of creating the slides and stuff. However, when I open the Impress file
on the Windows computer in the classroom stuff doesn't work. The most
recent exasperation was a video file in .mov format. So I finally
decided that it would be a lot easier just to hook up my T61 with Hardy
and run it directly.

But that turns out to be a lot of trouble also. I use the
nvidia-glx-new driver, so I installed nvidia-settings as well. If I run
it as root I can click on "Detect Displays" and it finds the projector
in the classroom. I can even get the projector to display my laptop.
That is, part of it. My laptop runs at 1680 x 1050 and I need every
pixel of it. But the nvidia-settings utility sees the projector as only
640 x 480. All that I see of my desktop is a small corner. 

How can I cram 1680 x 1050 into a device that only does 640 x 480?

I have a feeling that I have overlooked something basic.

I should also add that the nvidia-settings utility is confusing because
I don't understand what the options mean. E.g., what is the difference
between "twinview" "separate X screen"? It's kind of clunky too. Some
changes require restarting X. I do so by Ctrl-Alt-Backspace, and when I
log back in again I discover that my Gnome panel settings are gone.

I know there are people here who connect laptops to projectors at PSU
all the time. Is there a simple, reliable method that even a dummy like
me can figure out?

From joeniski at easystreet.net  Mon May 19 21:29:33 2008
From: joeniski at easystreet.net (Joe Shisei Niski)
Date: Mon, 19 May 2008 21:29:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Thinkpad, nVidia, External Monitor, and PSU
In-Reply-To: <20080519182808.bab5db27.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080519182808.bab5db27.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <483253AD.1090406@easystreet.net>

Does the projector have its own resolution settings - unless it's really 
old, it should offer a few better resolutions - at least 1280x1024. 
These settings are typically controlled by hardware buttons on the 
projector. Try setting the reolution on the project, then booting your 
laptop while connect - nvidia-settings should see it then. You only need 
to run nvidia-settings as root if you want to save the changes to your 
xorg.conf file.

Unless it's a high-end projector, i don't think it will offer 
wide-screen resolutions like your laptop.

________________________________________
Joe Shisei Niski
Portland, Oregon, USA

On Mon, 2008-05-19 at 19:04 -0700, Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
> >>>>> "Alan" == Alan  <alan at clueserver.org> writes:
> 
> Alan> It is not a joejob..  It is spam with your address as the return address.
> 
> Within the spam-fighting community, those are not distinct.

Clarity is not a feature of large parts of the spam fighting community I
am sad to say.

I have been on the wrong end of too damn many anti-spam twits who got a
piece of spam on a mailing list (like say p5p) and then complained to
the isp of the person running the mailing list and his isp and so on.
(Without checking if it actually came from there or not.)

Too many people say "something must be done! This is something,
therefore we must do it!" without figuring out what effect it will have
if they do.

There is a big difference between a joe job and backscatter.  I have
seen enough of each to be able to tell the difference.

odd though it may seem from my lack of cluefulness, I admin a few mail

> The term is "backscatter" when you are not being deliberately targeted

I'm working my way through the LDA (deliver) that dovecot offers and 
it's filtering (sieve, etal.) option.  Currently I use procmail but 
there appears to be better/easier using deliver.

Well back to topic.

> First I try to determine if the message is some sort of bounce, legit
> or not. If so then I try to find the original message either as an
> attachment or in-line. In-line is most difficult because there are so
> many different formats with different delimiters. But if I can find the
> original message then I run it through the regular spam filters. If it
> turns up positive then I assume my bounce is backscatter.
> 
> If I can't find the original message then I just search the body of the
> bounce message for the original "From:" line. If I find it I pick out the
> name and address. Say for example it's this:
> 
>    From: "go large" <myaddress at example.com>
> 
> (I also check the "From:" line even in the cases where I do find the
> entire attached message, but where it passes the regular spam filters.)
> 
> The backscatter clue is that the string "go large" does not match
> the string "Terry Griffin". [Insert your own joke here.] For myself and
> every user on my mail server I know their real name. I lookup the name
> using the email address from the "From:" line, then do a fuzzy string
> comparison between their name and whatever name is in the "From:" line.
> If there is no similarity then I assume the original message was spam
> with a forged address, and that the bounce is backscatter.

Great.  Worth doing on my personal email server and other places.

>    In my postfix header checks I have a line that looks like this:
> 
> /^Received: from nnn.nn.nnn.nn/                 reject Rule H9

Thanks Rich.  I'll have to see if the headers have same/similar Received 
lines.

> where the obfuscated part is my public IP address. Since my MTA will never
> receive mail from the outward-looking IP address I know it has to have been
