From griffint at pobox.com  Tue May 20 10:33:44 2008
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Tue, 20 May 2008 10:33:44 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] email joe-job.
In-Reply-To: <4833055E.1010905@acm.org>
References: <4831FCAE.6060606@acm.org><20493.198.102.182.114.1211243019.squirrel@webmail.whiz.to>
	<4833055E.1010905@acm.org>
Message-ID: <22593.198.102.182.114.1211304824.squirrel@webmail.whiz.to>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> Terry Griffin wrote:
>>> Looks like I've had a joe-job done to my main email address.  Forged
>>> envelopes with my address as the Return-Path.  A couple of thousand
>>> bounce messages since around 10:30 this morning and still coming in.
>>>
>>> Any ideas as to how to identify that they are bounce messages.  Is
>>> there
>>> a postfix header check or a procmail recipe?
>>>
>>> I'll probably start with the Sender containing postmaster and dump that
>>> into a folder.  That should cut down on the pile I have to sort through
>>> so I don't miss a real message.
>>>
>>> Not sure Spam Assassin would help and this is a pretty old mail server,
>>> running postfix, that has never been compromised before, so installing
>>> Spam Assassin isn't a good option.  I'm working towards replacing it
>>> but
>>> not ready yet.  Looks like I might have to get ready sooner.
>>>
>>
>
> Been to long since I had to deal with any real serious problems.  Yes,
> odd though it may seem from my lack of cluefulness, I admin a few mail
> spools but they have been running so well I don't have to deal with or
> think about these issues.  The brain has been working other stuff so
> last used is first dumped.  :-)
>
>> The term is "backscatter" when you are not being deliberately targeted
>> but instead your email address is simply being abused.
>>
>> Dealing with backscatter has been my struggle for the past many weeks,
>> but I've more or less got it under control now with some custom filters
>> that I slapped together.
>
> How are you coding and calling them?

Everything starts out going through /etc/procmailrc, but from there
I invoke various other filters including Vipul's Razor, Bogofilter,
and SpamAssassin. For backscatter I wrote a custom python script
also invoked from /etc/procmailrc. If the custom script determines
the message is a bounce and it can find a message attachment then
it runs the attachment through Vipul's Razor etc. Sometimes it finds
only the headers of the original message. These too can be run
through SpamAssassin but are less useful to Bogofilter and completely
useless for Vipul's Razor. If the message gets through all that without
being flagged as backscatter then the custom script does the final check
using the "From:" header method I described before, if it can find the
"From:" header that is.

>
> I'm working my way through the LDA (deliver) that dovecot offers and
> it's filtering (sieve, etal.) option.  Currently I use procmail but
> there appears to be better/easier using deliver.
>
> Well back to topic.
>
>> First I try to determine if the message is some sort of bounce, legit
>> or not. If so then I try to find the original message either as an
>> attachment or in-line. In-line is most difficult because there are so
>> many different formats with different delimiters. But if I can find the
>> original message then I run it through the regular spam filters. If it
>> turns up positive then I assume my bounce is backscatter.
>>
>> If I can't find the original message then I just search the body of the
>> bounce message for the original "From:" line. If I find it I pick out
>> the
>> name and address. Say for example it's this:
>>
>>    From: "go large" <myaddress at example.com>
>>
>> (I also check the "From:" line even in the cases where I do find the
>> entire attached message, but where it passes the regular spam filters.)
>>
>> The backscatter clue is that the string "go large" does not match
>> the string "Terry Griffin". [Insert your own joke here.] For myself and
>> every user on my mail server I know their real name. I lookup the name
>> using the email address from the "From:" line, then do a fuzzy string
>> comparison between their name and whatever name is in the "From:" line.
>> If there is no similarity then I assume the original message was spam
>> with a forged address, and that the bounce is backscatter.
>
> Great.  Worth doing on my personal email server and other places.
>

If you always send your mail via your personal server then Rich's
solution of checking the Received header would be a lot easier. In
my case that's not an option since a lot of my users send mail from
other places via other mail servers.

Terry

>
> Thanks,
> Rod
> --
>> Some bounces unfortunately don't contain enough of the original spam
>> to work with. But even so I'm now getting a backscatter catch rate
>> well in to the 90% range and no false positives.
>>
>> Terry
>>

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Thu May 22 02:11:22 2008
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Thu, 22 May 2008 02:11:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wifi WEP security problems
In-Reply-To: <4a0b36aa0805212212v12b82595q5ff49e2109eccad6@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4a0b36aa0805212212v12b82595q5ff49e2109eccad6@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <da2207740805220211u78d9faa0gba3a81b7556d06cd@mail.gmail.com>

Hint: wpa_supplicant does not work with WEP, only with WPA.
You just configure it manually for WEP, with NetworkMangler (TM) or using
iwconfig in a terminal.
See man iwconfig for reference.

iwconfig wlan0 essid [SSID_NAME] key [WEP_KEY]

For NetworkMangler are you sure you are using the correct options?
IE use WEP Open or WEP Shared or etc, not WPA?

WEP and WPA are totally different protocols.

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Thu May 22 08:53:05 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Thu, 22 May 2008 08:53:05 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wifi WEP security problems
In-Reply-To: <da2207740805220211u78d9faa0gba3a81b7556d06cd@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4a0b36aa0805212212v12b82595q5ff49e2109eccad6@mail.gmail.com>
	<da2207740805220211u78d9faa0gba3a81b7556d06cd@mail.gmail.com>

> Hint: wpa_supplicant does not work with WEP, only with WPA.

Hmm... That's kinda odd because I use it with WEP all the time.  For
instance, see the configuration examples in: 
  http://hostap.epitest.fi/cgi-bin/viewcvs.cgi/hostap/wpa_supplicant/wpa_supplicant.conf?rev=HEAD

One excerpt:
-----8<--------
# Shared WEP key connection (no WPA, no IEEE 802.1X)
network={
	ssid="static-wep-test"
	key_mgmt=NONE
	wep_key0="abcde"
	wep_key1=0102030405
	wep_key2="1234567890123"
	wep_tx_keyidx=0
	priority=5
}
----->8--------

> You just configure it manually for WEP, with NetworkMangler (TM) or using
> iwconfig in a terminal.
> See man iwconfig for reference.
> 
> iwconfig wlan0 essid [SSID_NAME] key [WEP_KEY]


This is certainly an option as well.  Probably a good way to take one
moving part out of the picture.


Cheers,
tim


From agentdcooper at gmail.com  Thu May 22 12:36:37 2008
From: agentdcooper at gmail.com (agent dale cooper)
Date: Thu, 22 May 2008 12:36:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wifi WEP security problems
Message-ID: <eae8ea2a0805221236n7b0d4767h7bf1ee34757fc819@mail.gmail.com>

>
> I am at a lost why WEP 128-bit SSID should give me that much trouble.  Any
> help much appreciated...
>

IMHO, I suggest ya just go with a nice `n easy X-windows based WEP/WPA
all-in-one solution like one of the following;

wifi-radar @ http://wifi-radar.systemimager.org/
   -OR-
kwifimanager @ http://kwifimanager.sourceforge.net/

Sure, a bash script is easy to produce, and highly manageable :: this is a
sample of what I use for connecting my client to a WEP-encrypt'd access
point via a bash script when I'm not using kwifimanager (I am using a DLink
wireless PCI card, which slackware shows as having a "Atheros
Communications, Inc. AR5212 802.11abg" chipset);

</usr/sbin/WEP2w.sh>
#!/bin/bash
/sbin/ifconfig ath0 down
/usr/local/bin/wlanconfig ath0 destroy
/usr/local/bin/wlanconfig ath0 create wlandev wifi0 wlanmode sta
/sbin/ifconfig ath0 up
/sbin/iwconfig ath0 essid 2W10001 mode managed rate auto ap
00:18:3f:xx:xx:xx
/sbin/iwconfig ath0 key 42:28:xx:xx:xx
sleep 3
killall -15 dhcpcd 1> /dev/null 2> /dev/null
/sbin/dhcpcd -t 10 -d ath0

I like all the purty colors though, of kwifimanager   =)   Choose to do
whatever you like, that's the beauty about linux, eh? Good luck! ((The end
of that bash script pulls a DHCP IP address, just FYI.))

-peace-
agentdcooper at gmail.com

OK I stand corrected, but perhaps I should have said, wpa_supplicant isn't
*necessary* for WEP, unlike WPA, which you need wpa_supplicant.
The iwconfig tools have built in functionality for handling WEP, and thus
you wouldn't need to use wpa_supplicant, unless you are using scripts or
some sort of setup where you normally use WPA, and have wpa_supplicant
already setup, and want to connect to a WEP only WAP.

So with a WEP WAP without WPA you can use wpa_supplicant but don't need to.
Better?  ;)

And as Tim said it removes one more variable from the picture to narrow down
the problem.

We now return you to your tongue tied regularly scheduled programming...

On Thu, May 22, 2008 at 8:53 AM, Tim <tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org> wrote:

>
>
> > Hint: wpa_supplicant does not work with WEP, only with WPA.
>
> Hmm... That's kinda odd because I use it with WEP all the time.  For
> instance, see the configuration examples in:
>
> http://hostap.epitest.fi/cgi-bin/viewcvs.cgi/hostap/wpa_supplicant/wpa_supplicant.conf?rev=HEAD
>
> One excerpt:
> -----8<--------
> # Shared WEP key connection (no WPA, no IEEE 802.1X)
> network={
>        ssid="static-wep-test"
>        key_mgmt=NONE
>        wep_key0="abcde"
>        wep_key1=0102030405<http://wwwapps.ups.com/WebTracking/processInputRequest?sort_by=status&tracknums_displayed=1&TypeOfInquiryNumber=T&loc=en_US&InquiryNumber1=0102030405&track.x=0&track.y=0>
>        wep_key2="1234567890123"
>        wep_tx_keyidx=0
>        priority=5
> }
> ----->8--------
>
> > You just configure it manually for WEP, with NetworkMangler (TM) or using
> > iwconfig in a terminal.
> > See man iwconfig for reference.
> >
> > iwconfig wlan0 essid [SSID_NAME] key [WEP_KEY]
>
>
> This is certainly an option as well.  Probably a good way to take one
> moving part out of the picture.
>

>>>>> "Matt" == Matt McKenzie <lnxknight at gmail.com> writes:

>> Well, no one actually uses WEP at home do they?  I mean, it's
>> bloody pointless, so this would be the common setup, right?
>> 
>> **looks around the mailing list pointedly**

I am obligated by my position to say that the *best* mode is open.
That said, WEP does one thing well, it establishes that the owner of
the AP is *not* intending to share their connection.  WEP, apparently,
can be cracked in short order, though I've never bothered to try, but
if someone does so, it would be hard for them to argue that they did
it unintentionally.

I think all networks should be open and available to share, as
security guru Bruce Schneier does[1], but I defer to the node owner to
make that decision.  Sharing is the polite and neighborly choice.  If
you want to be a hoarding a**hole, that is absolutely your right.

Ask not what free wifi can do for you, ask what you can do for free wifi.

[1] http://www.wired.com/politics/security/commentary/securitymatters/2008/01/securitymatters_0110


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net


From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Fri May 23 22:28:46 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Fri, 23 May 2008 22:28:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wifi WEP security problems
In-Reply-To: <86fxs8xpee.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <4a0b36aa0805212212v12b82595q5ff49e2109eccad6@mail.gmail.com>
	<da2207740805220211u78d9faa0gba3a81b7556d06cd@mail.gmail.com>
	<20080522155305.GJ28142@sentinelchicken.org>
	<da2207740805232011n7fb170dcs469274e59d992cf7@mail.gmail.com>
	<20080524035128.GZ28142@sentinelchicken.org>
	<da2207740805232117p5e4db9eam19984ee9cebafb0e@mail.gmail.com>
	<86fxs8xpee.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <20080524052846.GB28142@sentinelchicken.org>

> WEP, apparently,
> can be cracked in short order, though I've never bothered to try, but
> if someone does so, it would be hard for them to argue that they did
> it unintentionally.

Yes, it's true there are legal protections here.  More on that in a moment.

> I think all networks should be open and available to share, as
> security guru Bruce Schneier does[1], but I defer to the node owner to
> make that decision.  Sharing is the polite and neighborly choice.  If
> you want to be a hoarding a**hole, that is absolutely your right.

Well I have nothing against altruism, but unfortunately there are always
those out there who will exploit the good will of others.  If you made
all of your wifi traffic cleartext, then there's always a chance that
your neighbor will spy on what you're doing.  (Or perhaps more likely,
the guy that compromised his windoze box will sniff all wireless packets
around to gather passwords.)  Some of us do things with our computers
that we'd rather not have known by our neighbors.

> Ask not what free wifi can do for you, ask what you can do for free wifi.

I have often had the desire to set up a secondary open network with
reasonably throttled bandwidth for just this purpose.  Maybe sometime
you could help me out with that.

There are some legal worries I have about this though.  As you mentioned
above, having a locked down wireless network implies that you don't want
others snooping around on it.  If you could actually ever catch someone
cracking it (which is highly unlikely), then you would have some legal
recourse.

On another legal point, if you open up your wireless network to share
with others, you immediately become a provider under federal laws such
as the ECPA, Wiretap act, and Pen registers tap/trace act.  In all
technicality, just running tcpdump on that network could be risky.
You'd certainly want all users to agree to monitoring and other terms to
provide the proper exceptions.  If you aren't set up to do this, (as most
home users wouldn't be) then there would be some risk associated with
providing access to the neighbors.

Comments?

tim

	<20080524035128.GZ28142@sentinelchicken.org>

	<86fxs8xpee.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<20080524052846.GB28142@sentinelchicken.org>
	<86od6wvv7q.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <20080524163631.GB2419@sentinelchicken.org>

> Not always.  Often there are not.  E.g. my node has been providing
> access to something like 20 of my neighbors a day.  Rarely (like, none
> in at least the last 6 months) have abused the network.  And were they
> to, I have the means to teach them better behavior.

I'm sure it does work out well the vast majority of the time.  I just
don't want to have to deal with the feds showing up because someone was
using my connection to download child porn.

> Red herring.  Almost nothing (save maybe pop-mail) sends anything
> terribly sensitive over the air in the clear.  Any commerce traffic
> damn well better be encrypted end-to-end.  Any candy-ass wifi
> encryption isn't going to do you a damn bit of good once it's left
> your house.

It all depends on what you're doing I guess.  I just prefer not to have
to think about it.  I work from home and this changes the game a little
bit for me.

> Encrypted tunnels of various descriptions can help you out there.

Yeah, I guess end-to-end encryption is always better for security, but
it is a lot of work to maintain for each protocol if your goal is simply
to secure your wireless.

> If your network is open, no one is cracking your network.  If they are
> messing with your boxes, there are already laws against that.

Well sure, but there is essentially no way for the average person (and
rarely even for experts) to catch someone who is "cracking your
network".  Computer crimes are anonymous crimes.  Attackers have to be
really dumb to get caught.


> I am not a lawyer, but I have yet to find or hear of any significant
> risk from any statute for making your wifi network open.  If there
> were, don't you think someone would have already cracked down hard on
> some poor coffee shop owners?

Basically the general rule is, you can't snoop on others communications
at all, even if it's on your own network, unless you fall under a
specific exception clause provided by these laws.  This presentation
does an OK job of outlining things:

  http://www.blackhat.com/presentations/bh-usa-02/bh-us-02-eckinwiler-patriot.ppt

(blech, powerpoint, I know...)

Anyway, there are lots of exceptions that permit one to sniff traffic
for specific reasons or in specific situations, but these exceptions
change significantly once you become a public provider.  Once you start
providing access to the general public (and not just your own company or
your family), you have different legal obligations under these laws.
And yes they apply to everyone.

Of course there's not going to be a "crackdown" unless specific people
have complaints about you monitoring things.  However, think of the
silly lawsuits where burglars injure themselves on something while
breaking into your house.  Kinda inane stuff like that can come up.

Not trying to scare anyone away from offering public wifi, but these are
things to think about.  If you get all of your users to agree to all
monitoring, etc for any reason, then you probably don't have to worry
about it.  But of course IANAL... I'm just an investigator who has to
think about this stuff.

cheers,
tim

applying to criminal investigations and disclosure of private
information to the government.

I read the following without remotely feeling scared:

  http://ilt.eff.org/index.php/Privacy:_Wiretap_Act

Here is the actual law as written:

  http://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/html/uscode18/usc_sup_01_18_10_I_20_119.html

Again, IANAL, but I don't find anything in there particularly scary.
Specifically, look for the phrases: "It shall not be unlawful under
this chapter".

The main lesson, as it relates to the operator of an open wireless
network, seems to be that you should not randomly intercept third
party communications and disclose them.  I personally would not have
been inclined to do that anyway.  Otherwise, as far as I can tell,
there isn't much else to be concerned about.  You can monitor your
network in order to protect it or make sure its working, afaict.
Those tend to be the reasons I run tcpdump.

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Tue May 27 15:08:18 2008
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Tue, 27 May 2008 15:08:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS resolution for blacklisting...
Message-ID: <20080527150818.qeky10f4iss4o40w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

I'm working on a perl script that checks to see if the rcpt from and  
the mail from are resolveable.  If neither of these resolve, the rcpt  
from IP should be blacklisted.  Well, I've run into some questions  
about what resolved should mean.

[michael at eagle block_smtp]$ host aeincgr.com
aeincgr.com mail is handled by 15 mail2.aeincgr.com.
aeincgr.com mail is handled by 5 mail.aeincgr.com.

Look at the latter example here.  There is no A record.  Should I  
consider aeincgr.com resolved?

The following is my dns query script in my perl script I'm working on.  Notice
that only hostnames which produce an A record are considered resolved.

###### dnsquery
#  
-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=
sub dnsquery
{ # Return 0 if $teststr resolves or has resolved before.
   # Return 1 if $teststr doesn't resolve or it hasn't resolved before.
   # $teststr passed in.

     if ( $PRINT_FUNCTION )
     {
          print FUNCTRACE "dnsquery...\n\n";
     }

     # Make sure $teststr hasn't resolved already before checking it.
     if ( exists $dnsres{$teststr} )
     {
          $dnsres{$teststr}++;

          return 0;
     }

     # Make sure $teststr hasn't failed to resolve already before checking it.
     if ( exists $dnsdnres{teststr} )
     {
          $dnsdnres{$teststr}++;

          return 1;
     }

     use Net::DNS;

     my $res = Net::DNS::Resolver->new;

     my $query = $res->search($teststr);

     if ($query)
     {
         # Add to the dns resolved hash table.
         $dnsres{$teststr} = 1;

         foreach my $rr ($query->answer)
         {
            next unless $rr->type eq "A";
            print $teststr, ": ", $rr->address, "\n";
         }

         return 0;
     }
     else
     {
          # Add to the dns does not resolve hash table.
          $dnsdnres{$teststr} = 1;

          print $teststr, " does not resolve.", "\n";

          return 1;
     }

} # End of dnsquery.
#  
-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=

Should I check to see if MX records are returned instead of A records???  What
changes do I need to make to this function to do that???

      Michael C. Robinson

From jhoblitt at ifa.hawaii.edu  Tue May 27 13:53:57 2008
From: jhoblitt at ifa.hawaii.edu (Joshua Hoblitt)
Date: Tue, 27 May 2008 10:53:57 -1000
Subject: [PLUG] DNS resolution for blacklisting...
In-Reply-To: <20080527150818.qeky10f4iss4o40w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20080527150818.qeky10f4iss4o40w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <20080527205357.GE18155@ifa.hawaii.edu>

You probably want to follow the behavor of SMTP as defined in RFC 2821.  I
quote:

    5. Address Resolution and Mail Handling

       Once an SMTP client lexically identifies a domain to which mail will
       be delivered for processing (as described in sections 3.6 and 3.7), a
       DNS lookup MUST be performed to resolve the domain name [22].  The
       names are expected to be fully-qualified domain names (FQDNs):
       mechanisms for inferring FQDNs from partial names or local aliases
       are outside of this specification and, due to a history of problems,
       are generally discouraged.  The lookup first attempts to locate an MX
       record associated with the name.  If a CNAME record is found instead,
       the resulting name is processed as if it were the initial name.  If
       no MX records are found, but an A RR is found, the A RR is treated as
       if it was associated with an implicit MX RR, with a preference of 0,
       pointing to that host.  If one or more MX RRs are found for a given
       name, SMTP systems MUST NOT utilize any A RRs associated with that
       name unless they are located using the MX RRs; the "implicit MX" rule
       above applies only if there are no MX records present.  If MX records
       are present, but none of them are usable, this situation MUST be
       reported as an error.

ftp://ftp.rfc-editor.org/in-notes/rfc2821.txt

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Tue May 27 15:55:26 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Tue, 27 May 2008 15:55:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DNS resolution for blacklisting...
In-Reply-To: <20080527150818.qeky10f4iss4o40w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20080527150818.qeky10f4iss4o40w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <20080527225526.GF7306@sentinelchicken.org>


> I'm working on a perl script that checks to see if the rcpt from and  
> the mail from are resolveable.  If neither of these resolve, the rcpt  
> from IP should be blacklisted.  Well, I've run into some questions  
> about what resolved should mean.

Hmm, not sure you'd want to bother much with From: addresses in the
body.  If you do, you'd still end up having to accept the full email for
processing (there isn't a way for the server to prematurely quit a DATA
command, is there?) and parsing bodies is typically tricky.

> [michael at eagle block_smtp]$ host aeincgr.com
> aeincgr.com mail is handled by 15 mail2.aeincgr.com.
> aeincgr.com mail is handled by 5 mail.aeincgr.com.
> 
> Look at the latter example here.  There is no A record.  Should I  
> consider aeincgr.com resolved?

As for envelope from addresses, yes you definitely should be considering
these as real.  Go back through the RFCs and make sure you understand
how a mail server looks up the IP address to forward mail based on a
recipient's domain.  Hint: it doesn't start with an A record lookup.  A
base A (or AAAA) record lookup is a last resort method to get mail to
the destination.  MX records are definitely preferred and you should
honor these.

Of course if you're looking up MX records as you should be, do you now
follow those to ground with A/AAAA lookups?  What if you encounter a
CNAME?  What about lookup loops?  Things could get hairy if someone was
wanting to screw with you.  If it were me, I'd probably just let the
mail through if an MX or A or AAAA record existed for the base domain.

I do still question the overall spam reduction this is going to provide
though...  How much of your spam really comes from nonexistent domains?

good luck,
tim

I'm processing my postfix mail log for 554, User unknown in relay
recipient table...  I'm not writing a full blown mail server like
my older brother.  I figure the envelope from address should be
trustworthy and the mail from address really shouldn't be fake.
I'm looking at more than this of course.  I'm looking at the number
of times the mail to address is not one of my retired addresses 
by IP address.  If 3 times the same IP address tries a nonexistent
recipient, I blacklist it.  If 6 times a retired recipient is tried,
I blacklist.

Look at my comments header for the script:
#!/usr/bin/perl -w
#
# Look at the postfix mail log for smtp clients that need to be 5xx
# blocked.
# Specifically, focus on 550 smtp errors where the intended recipient is
# invalid.
#
# Be nice if the invalid recipient was valid once upon a time.
#
# Block anyone who tries to send to an unsupported domain 1 time.
# Block someone who tries to reach a phased out email address 6 times.
# Block someone who tries to reach a never existing email address 3 
# times.
#
# So what is the definition of someone that I should use?
#
#     Someone is an IP address.  You fail to be someone if none of your
#     provided identity information resolves.  Someone doesn't attempt 
#     to
#     reach a non existent recipient that was never phased out more than
#     3 times.  If an IP tries to reach a phased out recipient, that IP
#     get's 6 tries before it gets blacklisted.
#
#     Blacklist if RCPT from address, and mail from domain don't   
#     resolve.
#     No mail relay has any business letting mail through that is
#     totally unidentifiable.
#
#     Blacklist immediately IP that tries to reach a domain that isn't 
#     served.
#
# I hope noone can embed <,>, [, ], or : in their helo name or email 
# address.

The following is my new dnsquery.  I know that Rogan is suggesting
a combined data structure, but my Perl is weak and I understand
simple hashes better than multi level hashes.  I'm debating about
going to the point of checking mail exchangers against the rcpt
from IP address.

###### dnsquery
# 
-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
sub dnsquery
{ # Return 0 if $teststr resolves or has resolved before.
  # Return 1 if $teststr doesn't resolve or it hasn't resolved before.
  # $teststr passed in.

    $PRINT_DNS=1;

    if ( $PRINT_FUNCTION )
    {
         print FUNCTRACE "dnsquery...\n\n";
    }

    # Make sure $teststr hasn't resolved already before checking it.
    if ( exists $dnsres{$teststr} )
    {
         $dnsres{$teststr}++;

         return 0;
    }

    # Make sure $teststr hasn't failed to resolve already before 
    # checking it.
    if ( exists $dnsdnres{teststr} )
    {
         $dnsdnres{$teststr}++;

         return 1;
    }

    use Net::DNS;

    my $res = Net::DNS::Resolver->new;

    my $query = $res->search($teststr);

    if ($query)
    {
        # Add to the dns resolved hash table.
        $dnsres{$teststr} = 1;

        if ( $PRINT_DNS )
        {
             foreach my $rr ($query->answer)
             {
                next unless $rr->type eq "A";
                print $teststr, ": ", $rr->address, "\n";
             }
        }

        return 0;
    }
    else
    {    # Check for mail exchangers for the domain...
         my @mx = mx($res,$teststr);

         if (@mx)
         {
             foreach $rr (@mx)
             {
                    print $rr -> preference, " ", $rr -> exchange, "\n";
             }

             return 0;
         }
         else
         {
 warn "Can't find MX records for $teststr: ", $res -> errorstring, "\n";

             # Add to the dns does not resolve hash table.
             $dnsdnres{$teststr} = 1;

             return 1;
         }
    }

} # End of dnsquery.
# -=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-



Oh, and the following is the nevercheck function...
###### nevercheck
# -=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
sub nevercheck
{ # The idea is to catch strings that will never DNS resolve.
  # Returning a 0, don't do a DNS check.
  # If a 1 returned, DNS checking is OK.
  #
  # $teststr is passed in.

  if ( $PRINT_FUNCTION )
  {
       print FUNCTRACE "nevercheck...\n\n";
  }

  my @subdtststr=(""); # For testing the length of the DNS labels.
  my $LABEL_MAX=63;
  my $curr_lab="";

  my $PRNTDNCERR=1; # For debug output from nevercheck (1=on, 0=off).

  # Here are some strings which will never 
  # resolve. 
  my @rejstr=("localhost","unknown","localhost.localdomain");

  foreach $lookey (@rejstr)
  {

       if ( $lookey=~/^\Q$teststr\E$/ )
       {    ########## TEST ##########################  
            if ( 1 == $PRNTDNCERR )
            {
           print "nevercheck: $teststr on the unresolveable list...\n";
            }
            ##########################################
            return 0;
       }
  }

  # Acoording to RFC 1035, a Fully Qualified DNS name is 255 octets or 
  # less.
  my $tststrlen=length($teststr);

  if ( $tststrlen > 255 )
  {    ########## TEST ##########################################       
       if ( 1 == $PRNTDNCERR )
       {
   print "nevercheck: $teststr failed FQDN max 255 octets test...\n";
       }
       ##########################################################
       return 0;
  }

  # According to RFC 1035, a DNS label is 63 octets max.
  @subdtststr=split(/\./,$teststr);

  foreach $curr_lab (@subdtststr)
  {

          if ( length($curr_lab) > $LABEL_MAX )
          {
               ########## TEST ##########################               
               if ( 1 == $PRNTDNCERR )
               {
         print "nevercheck: DNS label $curr_lab too long...\n";
               }
               ##########################################
               return 0;
          }

  } # End of foreach loop.

  # Limit to allowable characters...  
  if ($teststr=~/^[0-9A-Za-z][0-9A-Za-z-]{0,62}\..*[\.]{0,1}/)
  {
      return 1; # Assume that $teststr is DNS queryable.
  }
  else
  {
      return 0;
  }

} # End of nevercheck.
# -=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
The purpose of the latter function is to prevent DNS checks when they
don't make sense.  Question is, am I right about the rules for DNS
names?  Are there more rules than this that I can use?

There is a function to update a history file, the main part of the
script, an initialization section for globals, a function to create
and append to a blacklist, and a process_record function.

The following is the main part of the script:
$mailfile="maillog";
$badstr=": Recipient address rejected: User unknown in relay recipient
table";
$TEST_PRINT=0;
$PRINTSEP=1;

# Open the maillog file in read mode.
open ( INDAT, "$mailfile" );

$trouble_line=<INDAT>; # Throw out the header line.

while ($trouble_line=<INDAT>)
{ # Pull from $mailfile all lines that match $badstr into 
  # $trouble_line...

       if ( $PRINT_FUNCTION )
       {
            print FUNCTRACE "MAIN PROGRAM...\n\n";
       }

       if ( $trouble_line=~/\Q$badstr\E/ )
       {    #**********************************************             
            # Test print the trouble line...
            if ( $TEST_PRINT )
            { print "\n\nMAIN PROGRAM trouble_line:$trouble_line\n\n"; }
            # END of Test print the trouble line...
            # **********************************************

            #************************************************
# From a trouble line extract the client helo, client RCPT From,
# and client ip address.

      # Extract the helo/ehlo address from the original $trouble_line.
            $helofrom=$trouble_line;
            $helofrom=~s/^.*;.*> proto=.* helo=<//;
            $helofrom=~s/>.*//;
            chomp ($helofrom);

            # Extract client RCPT from...
            $rcptfrom=$trouble_line;
            $rcptfrom=~s/^.*RCPT from //;
            $rcptfrom=~s/\]: 550 .*//;

     # Before finishing $rcptfrom, extract the client ip address...
            $rcptfip=$rcptfrom;
            $rcptfip=~s/^.*\[//;

            # Take ip address off of $rcptfrom...
            $rcptfrom=~s/\[[0-9.]*//;
            chomp ($rcptfrom);
            chomp ($rcptfip);
            # *********************************************************

            # ***********************************************
            # From the same trouble line, extract the message from and 
            # message to lines as well.

            # Extract the mail from address from the original 
            # $trouble_line.
            $msgfrom=$trouble_line;
            $msgfrom=~s/^.*RCPT from .*\[[0-9.]*\]: 550 .*;//;
            $msgfrom=~s/ proto=.* helo=<.*//;

            # Extract the mail to address from the current $msgfrom.
            $msgto=$msgfrom;
            $msgto=~s/ from=<.*> to=<//;
            $msgto=~s/>.*//;

            # Finish extracting the mail from address.
            $msgfrom=~s/ from=<//;
            $msgfrom=~s/> to=<.*//;

            chomp ($msgto);
            chomp ($msgfrom);
            # ***********************************************

# **********************************************************************
# Storing the: helo address, RCPT from address, client ip address,
# message from address, message to address.

           @badrec=( $helofrom, $rcptfrom, $rcptfip, $msgfrom, $msgto );
# **********************************************************************

#******************************************************************            
# Process this record before the next one is retrieved.

            process_record(@badrec);
#******************************************************************

            #
******************************************************************
# Test print after completing breakdown for a single record.
            if ( $PRINTSEP )
            {
print
"---------------------------------------------------------------------
\n";            }
   # END of Test print after completing breakdown for a single record.
           # **********************************************************
       } # Done acting on found match to $badstr.

} # Exit this bracket when INDAT ($mailfile) is fully parsed.

close (INDAT); # This is supposed to be an automatic feature of perl.

# The rest prints out the hashes and other statistics...

print "\n","########## The failed to dns resolve hash. ##########","\n";

while ( ($temp_name, $temp_times) = each %dnsdnres )
{
         print $temp_name, "\t", $temp_times, "\n";
}

print "\n","########## The resolved dns hash. ##########","\n";

while ( ($temp_name, $temp_times) = each %dnsres )
{
         print $temp_name, "\t", $temp_times, "\n";
}

print "\n","########## The invalid recipient hash ##########","\n";
while ( ($temp_ip, $temp_times) = each %invrechash )
{
         print $temp_ip, "\t", $temp_times, "\n";
}

print "\n","########## The valid recipient hash ##########","\n";
while ( ($temp_ip, $temp_times) = each %valrechash )
{
         print $temp_ip, "\t", $temp_times, "\n";
}

print "The total records processed: $records_processed\n";
print "The blacklisted records: $blacklisted\n";

close (FUNCTRACE);

This main part of the script tokenizes a $badstr so that I
can process it further.  I wrote this script to 80 columns,
but evolution is chopping it up funny when I paste it :-(
Perl's string manipulation power is very useful in this
part.

     Michael Robinson

On Wed, 28 May 2008 10:24:09 -0700
"Vincent Yau" <vyau5678 at gmail.com> dijo:

> I do not have a thinkpad but a dell laptop and
> Linksys WPC54G works fine for me.
> 
> I have also used E-Home EH103 USB adaptor and that works fine too
> (with Ubuntu 7 and 8).  I go through ndiswrapper in both cases.

I discovered that using ndiswrapper caused my battery to drain from a
full charge to nothing in less than an hour. That was with Gutsy
x86_64. One of the big deals about Hardy is that it was a stated goal
for the 64-bit version to have the same power management capabilities
as the 32-bit version. Indeed, my screen now darkens after a few
seconds of non-use, where it did not before. There may be other goodies
inside that I have not noticed. So what I'm saying is that ndiswrapper
is frequently a choice, but may produce undesired side effects.

I had a similar problem with connections getting dropped, not so much
hanging as you describe. In my case it turned out to be a timeout on
some firewall or router between the client and the server. I fixed
this by using login_keepalived on the server end.

   http://www.ekenberg.se/projects/login_keepalived/

>> I feel like it's just a bit different if it actually rings.  There's such a
>> kinetic element to that clang.
> 
>    Timer and battery would do the trick.

Alas, it's not that simple.  The POTS ring voltage is 90 VAC @ 20 Hz. 
  There are various ways of generating an acceptable signal, but they 
require time and a bit of electronics expertise.  I'm guessing that 
Benjamin is short on both counts.

Benjamin, if you do manage to track down an Asterisk system, I'd 
recommend checking the specs of the line card to be sure it's capable 
of driving a traditional ringer.

galen

Benjamin Foote
Those were pretty simple devices actually - if I remember correctly, the 
central office/PBX sent a low voltage DC (5 volt?) charge down the line 
and the bell rang (magnetic clapper type bell) - or isn't your's that 
old (they did make some nostalgic stuff later)?  If it is what I think 
it is, you could just use a timing device to periodically close the 
circuit for the length of time desired for the ring.  One could test the 
theory with DC batteries first - starting with 1.5 volt and working up 
until the desire ring was achieved.  Hope this helps, but even if it 
doesn't, thank you for reminding that such things did exist - memories, 
ya'know.
Regards
Fred James

http://www.linuxdevices.com/articles/AT2005548492.html

Now if you don't mind doing your own, there are a few projects that I
would recommend.

LEAF http://leaf.sourceforge.net/
IPCOP http://www.ipcop.org/
Untangle http://www.untangle.com/

There is also a package called firestarter that installs on most distro that
can be used as firewall.  At one of my companies they just installed
Fedora Core 4 and loaded firestarter to build the firewall.
The then added a vpn tunnel to the home office across the internet and
across the country.

On Thu, May 29, 2008 at 2:40 PM, Benjamin Foote <plug at bnf.net> wrote:
> I'll have to check for sure once I get home but I'm pretty sure this is the
> model...
>
> http://cgi.ebay.com/Vintage-Black-Desk-Bell-Western-Electric-Rotary-Phone_W0QQitemZ360056088441QQcategoryZ38038QQcmdZViewItem
>
> It does have a magnetic clanger.
>
> I'd be happy to hoist pints with anyone who could help me out.
>
> Could someone give me a ballpark estimate of how long it would take me to
> piece an appropriate asterisk server together myself?  Which digium card /
> other FXO solution would I require?

  I'm not clear on why you're building an Asterisk server just to ring
a bell?  The Black Magic module from Cambridge Labs is designed to
ring telephone bells.  It take in 12 vdc on one side, and puts out 86
vac @ 20 Hz ring signal on the other.

  You could easily gin up a interrupted ring signal with a Black Magic
module, a scrap PC, a relay, and a small perl script.  See Fig. 13 in
the "Black Magic! Telecom Design Tricks" manual (page 19) for an
example schematic.

Cambridge Labs - Engineering Design Literature
http://www.camblab.com/lit/lit.htm

  You will need an FXS card if you choose to build an Asterisk box.
The FXO interface emulates a telephone.  The FXS interface emulates a
Central Office.  The FXS provides the battery, dial tone, and ring
voltage which is used by the telephone (station).

  Many of the new FXS card (and PBXs) may not be able to drive a old
mechanical ringer.  The new systems don't always provide enough
current to saturate the bell coils.  It would be worth while to
contact the manufacture of the card and verify that it can drive an
official "REN: 1 Bell" Weco-500 style telephone.

Steve D...

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sat May 31 11:16:57 2008
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 31 May 2008 12:16:57 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] lisp: coerce string to real
Message-ID: <18497.38425.75901.295398@localhost.localdomain>

Any lispers out there (attn. Russ) who know a good way to coerce a
string (it's a given that the string is a representation of a legal
real value) to a real?  Common lisp provides (parse-integer) only.  I
think the following algorithm would work:

- split the string on the decimal point

- (parse-integer) the absolute value of the whole portion

- (parse-integer) the fractional portion

- divide that by the power of 10 that matches the number of decimal
  places to the right of the decimal point

- add that to the whole portion

- apply the sign of the original number

Is there a better way?

Carlos Konstanski

From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)

In-Reply-To: <18497.38425.75901.295398@localhost.localdomain>

Carlos Konstanski writes:
 > Any lispers out there (attn. Russ) who know a good way to coerce a
 > string (it's a given that the string is a representation of a legal
 > real value) to a real?  Common lisp provides (parse-integer) only.  I
 > think the following algorithm would work:
 > 
 > - split the string on the decimal point
 > 
 > - (parse-integer) the absolute value of the whole portion
 > 
 > - (parse-integer) the fractional portion
 > 
 > - divide that by the power of 10 that matches the number of decimal
 >   places to the right of the decimal point
 > 
 > - add that to the whole portion
 > 
 > - apply the sign of the original number
 > 
 > Is there a better way?
 > 
 > Carlos Konstanski
 > _______________________________________________
 > PLUG mailing list
 > PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
 > http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

Never mind, got 'er:

(defun cast-float (string-rep)
  "Tries to return the float representation of `string-rep'.  If
`string-rep' cannot be parsed as a float, returns `nil'."
  (let ((read-value (read-from-string string-rep)))
    (cond ((floatp read-value) read-value)
          ((integerp read-value) (float read-value))
          (t nil))))

Carlos Konstanski
